Re Dene: The Tribunal orders the name of the Respondent be
cancelled from the Register (if not already cancelled) at close of business
August 2010. Conditions apply in interim. The Respondent is not entitled to
reapply for registration for a minimum period of
3 years. Prohibition Order
made. Costs Order in favour of the HCCC (as set out in Orders).
Re Donnelly: The Tribunal orders the name of the Respondent be cancelled
from the Register at close of business on 6 August 2010.
Conditions apply in
interim. The Respondent is not entitled to reapply for registration for a
minimum period of 3 years. Tribunal
reserved on the question of Prohibition
Order. Costs Order in favour of the HCCC (as set out in Orders).

Introduction
Tribunal convened on 15 June 2009 to conduct an Inquiry into complaints made by
Ms Karen Mobbs, Director of Proceedings, Health
Care Complaints Commission
(‘the HCCC’), regarding two psychologists. The Complaint regarding
Ms Dene is dated 24 June
2008 and regarding Dr Donnelly dated 26 June 2008. The
Inquiry continued over 7 hearing days. Written submissions were received
thereafter.
As at date of this Decision, the Tribunal is awaiting further
submissions in the matter of Dr Donnelly on one issue.
Respondents were both engaged, in one capacity or another, in the psychology
department, of a Regional University in New South
Wales (Regional University).
Complaints arise from a report made by letter dated 9 May 2006 (Dene Volume 1,
exhibit 3) from the Program Director of a New South
Wales Pre-Trial Diversion of
Offenders Program. This program is a legislatively based service offered by the
Department of Health
for people who have been charged with sexual offences
against their children or their partner’s children.
summary the Complaints raised concerns regarding the
‘...failure of
mandated reporters to report child sexual abuse’
respect to the actual interventions used in relation to the father of the child
identified and the child herself’
. The Complaint also raised issues
surrounding the appropriate supervision of provisionally registered
psychologists.
are significant areas of agreement between each of the Respondents with respect
to the factual matrix in this matter. It is
common ground that Mrs A was
referred to Dr Donnelly for assessment as she was suffering from Multiple
Sclerosis. During this assessment,
Mr A, the husband of Mrs A, was identified as
being in need of psychological support. In summary, the support was directed to
Mr A to deal with the stress of his wife’s illness and rapidly
declining condition, her residing in a nursing home and consequentially
responsibilities as a ‘sole’ parent to two young daughters, Miss B
and Miss C.
Dene was given the responsibility of the care of Mr A. During the therapy
session Mr A made various disclosures to Ms Dene regarding
his conduct with Miss
B (then about 10 years of age). Ultimately, Ms Dene made a report to DoCS and Mr
A made admissions of extensive
sexual abuse of Miss A (and others) over a long
period of time.
matter raises many ethical questions. The Tribunal notes that there are numerous
source documents that provide psychologists
with guidance regarding ethical
matters. Section 19 of the Act provides for a Code to be developed by the Board
and further provides
that the Code should be observed by all psychologists.
There have been several editions of this Code and all registered psychologists
are bound by this Code. Further, documents with more specific content such as
the Australian Psychological Society
‘Guidelines on Reporting Child
Abuse and Neglect, and Criminal Activity’
published in November 2001
(and reformatted in 2004 - Dene - volume 2, exhibit 2 pages 163 - 169) provide a
clear and comprehensive
picture of the obligations placed upon registered
psychologists.

Relevant Law
Standard of proof
the standard of proof required to establish a complaint in this jurisdiction is
the civil standard, because of the seriousness
of the allegations and the
gravity of their consequences, the Tribunal must be ‘reasonably
satisfied’ that the particulars
of the Complaint have been
established.
[1938] HCA 34
(1938) 60 CLR 336
, Dixon J (as he then
was) at 362–363 said:
The seriousness of an allegation made, the inherent unlikelihood
of an occurrence of a given description, or the gravity of the consequences
flowing from a particular finding are considerations which must affect the
answer to the question whether the issue has been proved
to the reasonable
satisfaction of the tribunal. In such matters ‘reasonable
satisfaction’ should not be produced by
inexact proofs, indefinite
testimony, or indirect inferences ... This does not mean that some standard of
persuasion is fixed intermediate
between the satisfaction beyond reasonable
doubt required upon a criminal inquest and the reasonable satisfaction which in
issue may, not must, be based on a preponderance of probability. It
means that the nature of the issue necessarily affects the process
reasonable satisfaction is attained.
Polglaze v Veterinary Practitioners Board of NSW
[2009] NSWSC 347
the court confirmed that there is no third standard of proof i.e. one between
the civil standard and the criminal standard, rather,
because of the nature of
the matters in protective jurisdictions it was the quality and sufficiency of
the evidence that was required
to discharge the civil onus of proof.
Unsatisfactory professional conduct
25 of the Act is as follows:
Meaning of “unsatisfactory professional
For the purposes of this Act, "unsatisfactory professional conduct", in
relation to a registered psychologist, includes any of the
(a) any conduct that demonstrates that the knowledge, skill or judgment
possessed, or care exercised, by the psychologist in the practice
of psychology
is significantly below the standard reasonably expected of a psychologist of an
equivalent level of training or experience,
(b) a contravention by the psychologist of a provision of this Act or the
regulations or of a condition of the psychologist’s
registration,
(c) a failure without reasonable excuse by the psychologist to comply with a
direction by the Board to provide information with respect
to a complaint under
this Part against the psychologist,
(d) a failure by the psychologist to comply with an order made or a direction
given by the Board or the Tribunal under this Act,
(d1) a contravention by the psychologist of
section 34A (4) (Power of Commission to obtain information, records and
evidence) of
Health Care Complaints Act 1993
(e) any other improper or unethical conduct by a psychologist in the
course of the practice or purported practice of psychology.
definition of "unsatisfactory professional conduct" was amended by the
Registration Legislation Amendment Act 2004
(Schedule 1), which commenced on
1 March 2005. The amendment, in summary, added the reference to the standard
reasonably expected
of a psychologist of an equivalent level of training or
experience. The Particular of Complaint One relates to a period of time after
March 2005 and pursuant to the transitional provisions of the Statute Law
(Miscellaneous Provisions) Act (No. 2) (Schedule 4),
the relevant law to be
applied by this Tribunal is that which was in force at the time these Complaints
were filed. The Complaint
currently before the Tribunal is dated 10 September
2007. Consequently, the definition of ‘unsatisfactory professional
applicable in these proceedings is the definition contained in
the current legislation.
Unsatisfactory
professional conduct is found when the Tribunal is comfortably satisfied that
the conduct made out demonstrates one
of the deficiencies or inadequacies that
are set out in section 25. The Complaint currently before the Tribunal alleges
conduct particularised amounts to unsatisfactory professional conduct
within the meaning of this section – specifically sub-section
Board published a Code of Conduct dated 1 May 1990. This was superseded by the
Code of Ethical Conduct dated 3 November 1997,
which in turn was superseded by
the Code dated 1 November 2004 (which was provided to all registered
psychologists in June 2005).
Australian Psychological Society (APS) also publish a Code of Ethics and other
topic specific documents. As mentioned above, the
APS published a document
‘Guidelines on Reporting Child Abuse and Neglect, and Criminal
Activity’ in 2001. The Tribunal
notes that the CV of Dr Donnelly (HCCC
Donnelly Volume One – exhibit 8 – page 24) records he has been a
member of the
APS College of Clinical Neuropsychologists since 2001. In exhibit
RA Ms Dene provides a copy of her CV. Ms Dene lists Associate Membership
APS in this CV. In a letter to the University dated 20 September 2005, Dr
Donnelly makes reference not only to the relevant
child protection legislation
(Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act 1998)
but also to
the APS Code of Conduct and Reporting Guidelines.
Professional Misconduct
24 meaning of "professional misconduct":
For the purposes of this Act, "professional misconduct", in
relation to a registered psychologist, means unsatisfactory professional
of a sufficiently serious nature to justify suspension or cancellation of the
psychologist’s registration.
making the findings in relation to the Particulars and the Complaints the
Tribunal then needs to consider the appropriate Order(s)
to be made in the
circumstances.
dual reasons for this. In
Clyne v New South Wales Bar Association
[1960] HCA 40
104 CLR 186
at 201-202, the High Court said:
Although it is sometimes referred to as the ‘penalty of
disbarment’ it must be emphasised that a disbarring order is in
punitive in character. When such an order is made, it is made, from the public
point of view, for the protection of those
who require protection, and from the
professional point of view, in order that abuse of privilege may not lead to
loss of privilege
trust placed in psychologists is analogous, to that placed in medical
practitioners because of the necessity for the client to
disclose highly
personal and sensitive information to the psychologist in the course of
treatment. In a passage cited with approval
Health Care Complaints
Commission v Litchfield
[1997] NSWSC 297
(1997) 41 NSWLR 630
at 638, Priestley JA said in
Richter v Walton
(Court of Appeal, 15 September 1993, unreported):
The degree of trust which patients necessarily give to their
doctors may vary according to the condition which takes the patient to
doctor. Even in regard to the most commonplace medical matters, the trust a
patient places in a doctor is considerable. In
some cases, of which the present
seems to be an example, the patient’s trust cannot help but be almost
absolute. The doctor’s
power in regard to the patient in such cases is
also very great. I do not mean power in the abstract way but as a matter of
the extent of the power will vary according to the temperament of the
patient, but the doctor with some patients and for limited
periods, because of
the relationship in which they are temporarily placed, is in a position to do
whatever the doctor wants with
the body of the patient. This is one of the
reasons why doctors are subject to correspondingly great obligations and are
to maintain high standards; all this being very much in the public
characterising the conduct, the Tribunal has regard to the potential for harm of
the conduct rather then the actual harm that
eventuated.
this Tribunal and others exercising a similar jurisdiction in New South Wales
have always adopted the practice of considering
the particulars of complaint
separately or cumulatively when determining whether the conduct amounts to
unsatisfactory professional
conduct or professional misconduct, the Tribunal
notes the comments made in a decision of the Court of Appeal in New Zealand,
Duncan v Medical Practitioners Disciplinary Committee
(1986) 1 NZLR 513
where at 546, Cooke P, Richardson, McMullin and Somers JJ said:
... a case is conceivable in which over a period a practitioner
manifests in a diversity of ways, some more serious than others, such
disregard of his professional responsibilities that, viewed as a whole, his
conduct can rightly be described as disgraceful
in a professional respect... we
can see nothing in the Act or in natural justice to prevent the Committee, after
investigating a
range of complaints, from regarding a comprehensive charge is
appropriate as well as separate ones. Indeed it might be against the
interest to deny the Committee any right to present an all-embracing charge. It
may be important that the appropriate professional
tribunal should be able to
look at the practitioner’s whole attitude to practice.
Prohibition Orders
prohibition order may be made by the Tribunal in accordance with section 53,
subsection (3A) (an amendment introduced into the Act
on 4 December 2006). This
sub-section states:
If the Tribunal makes an order under subsection
(2) in respect of a person and it is satisfied that the person poses a
substantial
risk to the health of members of the public, it may by order (a
“prohibition order”) do any one or more of the following:
(a) prohibit the person from providing health services or specified health
services for the period specified in the order or permanently,
(b) place such conditions as the Tribunal thinks appropriate on
the provision of health services or specified health services by the
the period specified in the order or permanently.
service is defined in
Health Care Complaints Act 1993
including the following services, whether provided as public or private
(a) medical, hospital and nursing services;
(b) dental services;
(c) mental health services;
(d) pharmaceutical services;
(e) ambulance services;
(f) community health services;
(g) health education services;
(h) welfare services necessary to implement any services referred to in
paragraphs (a) - (g);
(i) services provided by podiatrists, chiropractors, osteopaths,
optometrists, physiotherapists, psychologists and optical dispensers;
(j) services provided by dieticians, masseurs, naturopaths, acupuncturists,
occupational therapists, speech therapists, audiologists,
audiometrists and
radiographers;
(k) services provided in other alternative health care fields;
(k1) forensic pathology services; and
(l) a service prescribed by the
Health Care Complaints Regulation
a health service for the purposes of this the
Health Care Complaints Act
‘Substantial’
interpretation of the word ‘substantial’ has been considered in a
number of cases. In
New South Wales Crime Commission v Vuletic and Others
[2005] NSWSC 614
at paragraph 53, Johnson J gave the following summary of
relevant law:
The word “substantial” is a word which may vary in
its meaning depending upon the context in which it is used and the
matter in relation to which it is to be applied: Director of Public Prosecutions
[1998] NSWSC 16
(1998) 44 NSWLR 618
at 622. In Tillmanns Butcheries Pty Limited v
Australasian Meat Industry Employees’ Union
[1979] FCA 85
(1979) 42 FLR 331
Deane J observed that the word “substantial” was not only
susceptible of ambiguity, but was a word calculated
to conceal a lack of
precision. In O’Brien Glass Industries Limited v Cool & Sons Pty
[1983] FCA 191
(1983) 77 FLR 441
, Fox J at 447 referred to Tillmanns Butcheries and
observed that, in selecting the word “substantial”:
“The legislature has chosen a word of intractably indefinite import
and much must be left to individual assessment.”
In Losurdo, it was said at 622 that the word
“substantial” is an ordinary English word and must be given its
meaning in the context in which it appears.
the purpose of
(3A) the Tribunal is of the view that
‘substantial’ requires something more than a bare risk but is less
stringent than
special, exceptional or unusual.
a prohibition order is made, and a de-registered practitioner provides a service
in contravention of the order the penalty imposed
section 10AK
Public Health Act 1991
is a maximum fine of $5,500.00 or 12 months
imprisonment or both.
Notification of Deregistration
de-registered health practitioner who is the subject of a prohibition order
must, before providing a health service to a person
ensure that notification is
made that the health practitioner was registered under a particular health
registration Act or corresponding
health registration legislation and that the
health practitioner’s registration under that Act or legislation has been
or suspended. Notification must be made to:
person to whom the health practitioner intends to provide the health service or,
if that person is under 16 years of age or
under guardianship, a parent or
guardian of the person,
(b) if the health service is to be provided by the health practitioner as an
employee, the health practitioner’s employer.
Failure to do so is an offence under
section 10AK
Public Health
and carries a penalty of a fine of up to $5,500.00.
Advertising of Health Services
person must not advertise a health service that is to be provided by a
deregistered health practitioner unless the advertisement
specifies that the
health practitioner was registered under a particular health registration Act or
corresponding health registration
legislation and that the health
practitioner’s registration under that Act or legislation is cancelled or
A person must not advertise a health service that is to
be provided by a health practitioner who is subject to a prohibition order
unless the advertisement specifies that the health practitioner is subject to
Failure to comply with either of these requirements is an offence under
section 10AL
Public Health Act
and carries a penalty of a
fine of up to $5,500.00.
Background material
Background of Ms Dene
Respondent was born in 1957. After completing her Higher School Certificate in
1975 she undertook a Diploma in Teaching (Primary),
graduating in 1979. Ms Dene
taught for three years before taking leave for family reasons. Between 1989 and
1993 Ms Dene worked as
a teacher from time to time. Ms Dene commenced a Bachelor
of Psychology (with honours) at the regional University in 1993 and graduated
2000. During this same period Ms Dene also trained as a family mediator and
counsellor and worked in this area on a sessional
basis. In 2002 Ms Dene
commenced a post graduate degree in Clinical Psychology at the regional
University. She suspended this course
in 2005 and has not returned thereto.
at the University, Ms Dene completed two practica. A 44 day practicum supervised
by Dr Donnelly commencing in February 2003
and completed by about August or
September 2003; the other was supervised by both Dr Donnelly and Dr
Brechman-Toussaint. Ms Dene
stated that in approximately early 2005 she
destroyed the ‘detailed notes’ she made of the supervision sessions.
Dene was granted conditional registration as a psychologist (i.e. she was
provisionally registered) on 2 April 2002. Full Registration
was granted on 5
April 2005. As at the time of this Decision, the Tribunal understands that Ms
Dene’s application to re-new
her Registration is pending.
leaving the Regional University, Ms Dene spent a short time in private practice
before commencing work as a counsellor for Alzheimer’s
Australia (on a
contract basis, one day per week) from October 2006 until August 2008. In
January 2007 until the time of the Inquiry
Ms Dene has worked delivering
‘psychological services’ at the Coffs Harbour Women’s Health
Centre (CV attached
to exhibit RA). Ms Dene initially started working one day
per week and this has increased to two to three days a week. Ms Dene stated
she worked part time as she had found the involvement in this case to be
stressful and part time work was all she could manage.
Ms Dene gave oral
evidence that she had received supervision by telephone once a week from a
counsellor while undertaking this part-time
work. In her oral evidence Ms Dene
indicated that she had restricted her work to patient’s that did not
involve any allegations
of child sexual abuse. Ms Dene indicated that she
limited her practice to seeing women for ‘
grief, anxiety, depression
and carer stress’
(T102, 18/6/09).
Background of Dr Donnelly
Donnelly was born in 1955 in the United States. After working as a steelworker
he joined the United States Air Force in 1974 where
he remained for four years.
In 1977 he commenced part-time work as a nursing assistant, then as a health
educator. He undertook academic
studies and graduated from the University of
California at Irvine with a Bachelor of Science (with Research Honours) in 1986.
2000 he completed a Master of Science and a Doctorate in Clinical Psychology at
Vanderbilt University. Dr Donnelly’s statement
records that he completed
five clinical practica comprising approximately 4000 supervised hours of work.
Dr Donnelly’s evidence
is that he had clinical experience at major clinics
in the United States during his training.
Donnelly’s evidence is (see paragraphs 6 to 9 of the statement dated 11
June 2009) that he had considerable experience working
with sex offenders and
victims of sexual abuse and provided a certificate acknowledging his attendance
at a one day seminar at the
Carondelet Management Institute titled ‘
to help Adult and Child Survivors of Sexual Abuse’
from 3 December
1997 (exhibit R1). His first
to the concept of
reporting sexual abuse was when he was working on the university campus and
sexual abuse was prevalent among fraternities
and sororities. His subsequent
experience included the use of biofeedback as interventions for sex offenders
and clinical work with
children abused by family members or clergy once he
became a licensed Marriage, Child and Family Counsellor in Tennessee (in 1992).
2001 to 2006 Dr Donnelly worked as a Lecturer and Clinical Neuropsychologist at
the School of Psychology, Clinical Training program
at the Regional University.
Dr Donnelly taught ethics (including the application of mandatory reporting
requirements in relation
to children – page 2, exhibit R4).
a few months in the early part of 2005 Dr Donnelly took medical leave as a
result of ‘
high stress and depression related to conditions in the
and then from about September 2005 he took study leave. In
April 2006 he was appointed as Head of Psychology at the Sydney Children’s
Hospital at Randwick.
the time of the Inquiry Dr Donnelly had left the Sydney Children’s
Hospital and was working part time at another regional
University and was
attempting to establish a private practice on the one day a week he was not
working at the University.
Tribunal notes that Dr Donnelly maintains his Registration as at the date of
this Decision.
Background to the Complaint
Dene provided treatment to Mr A from June 2003 to August 2005. Dr Donnelly was
the supervisor of Ms Dene at least between January
2003 and November 2004,
including as her supervisor for her work with Mr A.
noted above, allegations regarding the conduct of the Respondents were received
by the Board by way of a letter dated 9 May 2006
from Mr Dale Tolliday, Program
Director, Cedar Cottage, NSW Pre-Trial Diversion of Offenders Program.
matter was referred to the HCCC for investigation in the usual course and the
Complaints were referred to the Tribunal. The Reasons
for Decision in relation
to the concurrent hearing of the Complaints is found at
HCCC v Dene &
[2009] PSTNSW 7.
the Directions Hearing held on 7 May 2009, Counsel for Ms Dene advised that she
admitted the substance of the conduct that led
to her coming before the Tribunal
but did not admit that the conduct amounted to unsatisfactory professional
conduct or professional
misconduct. This was confirmed in the written
submissions dated 16 October 2009 made on behalf of Ms Dene. The Tribunal notes
a good deal of Ms Dene’s evidence did not sit with her making such
admissions as to the substance of the conduct, and that
the majority of the
Particulars were not ultimately admitted in Ms Dene’s final written
submissions.
Evidence – General Overview
large amount of evidence came before the Tribunal. The index (Annexure A) sets
out the documentary evidence. The transcript of the
oral evidence is lengthy
given the seven hearing days. A good deal of this evidence is contradictory.
While the entirety of the evidence
has been carefully considered, the Tribunal
does not consider a recount of the entirety of the evidence is required.
Evidence – Ms Dene
Brief Overview – Documentary Evidence Ms
15 September 2004, Ms Dene conducted a joint session with Mr A and Miss B. This
session is recorded in clinical notes made by Ms
Dene (Volume 2, exhibit 1 at
pages 29 and 30). This note records the following:
Session Goals:
1. To meet with [Miss B] and build some rapport with her
2. Use strength cards, and use these to prepare [Miss B] for the joint
3. Support [Miss B]
4. Contract with [Miss B] for future contact.
5. Confirm appointment with [Mr A] for Monday.
Behavioural Observations:
[Miss B] was dressed in her school uniform, and did not take her hat off
inside. She used her hat to cover her eyes or face when she
felt uncomfortable.
[Miss B] tried to avoid telling me she knew why she had come. She cried through
the session and curled up in
the chair, hiding her eyes. At the conclusion of
the session she was able to look at her father, and me briefly.
[Miss B] was outgoing and engaged in some eye contact with me. She was
able to talk about her strengths and love for horses in particular.
I told [Miss
B] I knew what her father had done. She hid behind her hat and did not want to
talk about it. When I indicated that
her father would join us to talk to her,
she said ‘no’. I spent some time letting her express her resistance
I talked to her about things hidden and about hiding away from things
that are painful. She cried. When [Mr A] came in he sat next
to her and stroked
her head and back. She appeared somewhat comforted by this, but did not uncurl
or make eye contact. [Mr A] apologised
to her, and told her he would not hurt
her again. After awhile, I talked to [Miss B] and told her about heroes, and
being scared
and about not wanting to face things. I talked to her about
strength, and about taking her time to make eye contact with her father.
talked about her strength coming through her eyes. We waited for some time.
[Miss B] changed positions in her chair, gradually
uncurled and made some brief
eye contact with her dad. I talked a little about feeling weird and normal; and
the sad and uncomfortable feelings; and about the benefits
of facing emotionally painful situations. I left [Miss B] and [Mr A] alone
she felt able to face the outside world again. When she came out of the room she
used her hat to avoid me.
I believe that [Miss B] took the first steps in healing the abuse today.
She did not want to and it was very uncomfortable and painful
for her. I was
pleased to see that she was able to regain her strength and in her own time make
did not expect her to understand the
significance of today. It will not be until she is older that it may make some
sense to her.
[Mr A] cried when he talked to [Miss B] about his regret for what he had
done. He was distressed by her distress. He was able to follow
my cue to be
silent and allow [Miss B] to set the pace of her regaining her composure.
The session allowed the expression of emotion in a real and unhurried way.
I recommended to [Mr A] that he and [Miss B] took their
time to settle and
adjust before returning to normal daily activities.
I was conscious that [Mr A] did not use specific language about touching
her inappropriately. I decided not to intervene, as I believed
that [Miss B] was
being confronted with very sensitive emotional material, and did not see any
benefit from increasing the intensity.
this point the Tribunal comments that this record indicates that Miss B did not
react in a positive fashion to this session, in
fact there are clear signs she
was extremely distressed by the intervention. There is nothing in the clinical
notes regarding the
psychological preparation of Miss B for the joint session
with her father, apart from a discussion regarding her ‘strengths
for horses in particular’. The Tribunal notes that at the time of this
interview Miss B was 10 years of age. Miss
B would have been a ‘vulnerable
person’ in many regards, particularly as she was prepubescent, had an
extremely ill parent
and had not been psychologically prepared for a session of
this nature. Even if the Tribunal only had regard to the record of the
which Miss B sat in the chair with her hat pulled over her eyes during the
session, the Tribunal would still be of the view
that this session was
inappropriate.

Other documentary evidence – Ms Dene
number of references were provided to the Tribunal on behalf of Ms Dene. Ms
Louise Litchfield provided a reference dated 30 March
2009 (exhibit RG). After
training as a teacher, Ms Litchfield has worked for many years in the area of
Mediation. Ms Litchfield states
that she has known Ms Dene since 1994 when they
both worked for ‘Interrelate’ as mediators. Ms Litchfield reports
Ms Dene was always focused and thorough in her attention to details
regarding clinical and service delivery. The reference continued:
In particular it was at Kerri’s insistence that
Interrelate developed a model of individual Intake Interviews for separated
parents to take account of client safety in the event of undetected issues of
family violence. This represented a significant change
and extension of the
mediation service. Kerri developed a strong rationale based on her thorough
research to argue for and establish
this important clinical change to the
Interrelate mode.
In light of the nature of the current complaint that has been referred to
the Psychologists Tribunal, it is also worth noting that,
in her role as
Mediation Clinical Coordinator, Kerri was particularly concerned that mediators
develop a child focused approach to
practice. It is my opinion that Kerri Dene
was abreast with the emerging vangard of clinical opinion at that time and which
as a significant trend in the field of Child Focused Family Dispute
Resolution. The priority Kerri placed on the welfare and care
of children
caught up in family breakdown and separation influenced the mediators under her
supervision to be more mindful of the
complexity of separation issues and the
needs of vulnerable family members in each case.
Ms Litchfield holds Ms Dene in high regard and has found her to be a diligent,
intelligent person who has observed professional
and personal boundaries.
reference was obtained from Associate Professor Neil Marshall (exhibit RJ).
A/Prof Marshall currently holds an academic appointment
at the Regional
University, having been an academic at various universities for the last 30
years. A/Prof Marshall reports that he
first met Ms Dene at a meeting of the
Armidale Jung Society in 2002. A/Prof Marshall reports that he has become good
friends with
Ms Dene through their common interest in the work of Carl Jung and
in philosophy and psychology generally.
Marshall noted that Ms Dene had also been a doctoral candidate at the Regional
University. He observed that she demonstrated
‘a very high degree of
discipline and determination to complete her studies and become a practising
psychologist’
reference continues:
In the course of our discussions over the years I have been left
in no doubt about depth and extent of Kerri’s interactive skills.
have been many occasions when she has assisted me most effectively me (sic) with
a problem or concern. Indeed, I have met
few people in my life with the
empathic abilities, or perceptive qualities, that she possesses. I have also
been inspired by her
enthusiasm for her work, and her commitment to the welfare
of her clients.
In the time I have known her, Kerri has always behaved with the utmost
integrity in both her personal and professional life. I cannot
imagine that she
would knowingly breach ethical guidelines, or behave in any way that would
adversely impact on her clients or colleagues.
I would like to draw attention to one matter in particular in
Kerri’s statement that stood out for me as being critical -the
relationship between the doctoral student and her supervisor. The nature of
this relationship is often not well understood in the
extramural context.
Doctoral candidates spend three or four years researching and writing the thesis
under the guidance of a supervisor.
Given the specialist content of a thesis,
and the extensive supervision required, a close relationship usually develops
between the
student and the supervisor. This relationship necessarily involves
a very high degree of trust, and dependency on the part of the
student. In fact
the success or otherwise of the thesis may be closely geared to the quality of
the relationship. In such situations
the word of the supervisor often becomes
law and goes unquestioned by the student. From my reading of Kerri’s
statement, I
suspect that something along these lines may have occurred in this
Marshall noted that Ms Dene was still contributing to her local community by
writing a fortnightly women’s health column
in a local newspaper.
reference dated 30 March 2009 was provided by Ms Holmes (exhibit RI). Ms Holmes
first met Ms Dene in 1994 while she was completing
a Mediators course with
Interrelate. Ms Holmes states that Ms Dene was described as the ‘diamond
of the trainee team’
as she had been singled out as the person with the
highest potential. Ms Holmes worked with Ms Dene until 2001. While they worked
together Ms Holmes observed Ms Dene to be highly principled and to have a
professional approach to her work. Ms Dene took her responsibilities
read widely and encouraged all members of staff to engage in on going education.

sex education to the child in question and emotional counselling to her and her

again, at no time did Dr Donnelly specifically seek my
advice/reassurance/opinion as to whether or not the case in question
that he make a report to the Director General of the Department of Community
Services, as required under the
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection)
. He did not ask my opinion as to whether or not I considered the
children to be at risk of harm either initially or subsequent to
my working with
the children. Rather he sought my professional involvement for the specific task
of providing psychosocial intervention
to children whom he himself had
determined to be "not at risk". I did not give advice/reassurance to Dr Donnelly
in relation to this case. If Dr Donnelly chose to use my
actions in accepting his "brief' as reassurance for the appropriateness
own decisions about the matter, I had no awareness of or control over
response to the second allegation, being failure to report to DOCS, Dr
Brechman-Toussaint referred to her letter dated 15 September
2005 to the Dean of
the facility at the University, enclosing a copy thereof.
conclusion, Dr Brechman-Toussaint notes the ‘
only clinical judgement
she was required to make in relation to this case was whether or not to accept
the brief from my colleague
to provide the requested service’.

Peer Review Reports
psychologists were called by the HCCC to give evidence by way of a peer review.
The Tribunal notes that some of the evidence given
by Ms Zandberg was also in
the nature of a peer report
Evidence of Ms Boland
Boland set out in her report the steps she considered appropriate for a
supervisor of a ‘student ‘ that had had a disclosure
of child sexual
assault made to them by a client. This would include looking at a realistic
definition of ‘current abuse’,
an explanation of the grooming
processes employed by paedophiles, associated issues of unreliability of their
reports, the potentially
self-serving nature of voluntary disclosures and the
identification of the client’s minimising language.
Boland stated that she would challenge the student’s thinking regarding
the possible negative consequences of a DoCS Helpline
report. Reminded the
‘student’ of the need for specialist (DoCS) assessment of
‘Risk of Harm’. The supervisor
also had a role in assisting the
student to differentiate between his/her empathy for the client as a carer of an
invalid wife and
the distress and discomfort the student is likely to have felt
at the disclosure of the abuse.
supervisor should then assist the student to prepare the relevant information
for the report and make a report of Risk of Harm
to the Helpline in the
student’s presence. The supervisor should then have instructed the student
that they should not inform
the client about the DoCS Report due to Duty of Care
issues related to both the victim and to the client and that further therapy
with this client should be delayed pending a DoCS decision regarding what action
should be taken.
Boland indicated she would then inform the student she would not be working with
the client in the future, would provide the ‘student’
reassurance indicating that the emotional response was entirely understandable
given her personal history and insist that she
seek some counselling (that day
if possible) from the Student Counselling Service. Ms Boland indicated that any
failure by the ‘student’
to seek sufficient professional counselling
and support would be viewed with concern as willingness and ability to engage in
ongoing process of self-monitoring and self-reflection is essential to
ongoing professional development. At some time in the near
future the supervisor
should arrange to meet with the student to reflect further on the experience and
to discuss any further professional
issues raised.
Boland expressed the view that the tentative disclosure made by Mr A should have
been viewed by his therapist with compassion and
the client’s subsequent
disclosure would immediately create a cognitive and emotional dissonance for any
therapist as to how
a therapist should respond to paedophilic behaviour. A
‘student’ was entitled to look to the supervisor for clear guidance
and education.
the heading of
‘The demands of contradictory therapeutic goals and
their implications for the student’s conceptual clarity’
Boland makes the following comments:
... even for highly trained specialist therapists it would be
impossible to provide the rigorous therapeutic challenges necessary
rehabilitation of a sexual abuser while also required to act as a compassionate
and uncritically supportive therapist in
relation to other matters. I’m
astonished this was expected of any experienced Clinical Psychologist let alone
the heading
Ms Boland wrote:
I believe she should have been provided with much better
supervision regarding her client’s disclosure of child sexual abuse
that as a student of the university she was entitled to rely on the expertise
and direction of University supervisors.
Regarding the student’s personal disclosure to the client, it
appears from Ms Dene’s explanation, that this was spontaneous,
reflective, response. Firstly, she had just heard information that shocked and
disturbed her. Secondly, having behaved in
what would be viewed by her as an
embarrassingly unprofessional manner she would most likely want to provide an
explanation to the
client for this. ...
I would not expect such an unrestrained response from a fully trained and
experienced clinician but I believe we should not be surprised
by the reactions
of a student – particularly one for whom such a disclosure by a client
held such significant personal resonance.
the heading
‘Therapeutic Goals’
Ms Boland wrote:
Even for highly trained specialist therapists it would be
impossible to provide the rigorous and at times the relentlessly confronting
therapeutic challenges necessary for the attempted rehabilitation of a sexual
abuser while also required to act as a compassionate
and uncritically supportive
therapist. I do not believe Ms Dene. should have ever been expected to perform
both roles.
I have no difficulty in believing that all these circumstances, which
eventually led to the complaints against Ms Dene, could have
been avoided had
she been exposed during her clinical training to sufficiently comprehensive
education and supervision.
Boland made the following comments with respect to the relevance of Miss
Dene’s personal history and intellectual abilities:
Apart from the immediate impact of the client’s disclosure
upon her, I can see no reason to rely upon Ms Dene’s personal
CSA as an explanation for her later conceptual confusion and actions. Neither
can I see any evidence that Ms Dene’s
innate capacity for abstract
reasoning and conceptual clarity were not up to the professional challenge of
clinical work –
she has a First Class Honours degree after all. Therefore
I can see no reason to believe that she would not have responded very well
adequate supervision and support had she received it.
answer to a question in chief regarding her opinion as to whether or not Ms Dene
should have been ‘extracted out of the process’

was cruel to expect her to do that.
... very brave person who asserted themselves ... overriding the direction
that they received from their clinic supervisor. I wouldn't
Boland then expressed the view that not only would Ms Dene have gained nothing
in terms of clinical skills or learning in the situation
but that it would be
‘very counter-productive’.
Ms Boland continued with the
description of the position in which Ms Dene was placed:
position where, on the one hand, you're expected to be
comforting and supportive of a client, and on the other hand to be sufficiently
rigorous and challenging to ensure that he's not being - he's not lying,
he's not deceiving, that he's not simply being self-serving,
or simply being
strategic, they're two - they are two diametrically opposed therapeutic
approaches. And if she was asked to do both,
I really don't see how on earth
she could do it. I can't think of anyone in our profession who could maintain
effectively both roles.

the first report regarding Ms Dene Dr Banks responded to a number of questions
posed by the HCCC. The first question asked by the
Whether Ms Dene’s care and treatment of Mr A under Dr
Donnelly’s supervision and independently, was appropriate, particularly
after the disclosure made by Mr A that he had inappropriately touched his
daughter, Miss K, during massages over a period of time.
response to this question Dr Banks noted that the:
...standard reasonably expected of the intern would have been to
assert herself sufficiently to be able to extricate herself from
the case. The
reasonable standard is that interns do not provide therapy without adequate
supervision as they are not fully registered
and cannot lawfully practise as
psychologists independently.
Banks opines at page 6 of 18 that ‘
the departure from the standards
described was
not just significantly but overwhelmingly below the reasonable
second question asked of Dr Banks was:
Whether Ms Dene disclosed her history of childhood sexual abuse
to Mr A such a disclosure was a transgression of professional boundaries
Banks described Ms Dene’s disclosure as
‘ill-considered, naive
and excessively familiar’
and that the
‘disclosure to Mr A of
her history of abuse represents a substantial transgression of professional
boundaries’
. Dr Banks re-iterated that
‘the standard
reasonably expected of the intern would have been to assert herself sufficiently
to be able to extricate herself
from the case’.
In addition Dr Banks
‘the standard reasonably expected of the supervisor would
have been to intervene, terminate the therapy, assist the intern to
seek therapy
for herself (from somebody else, and of course (in reverse priority) report the
matter to DoCS’.
Dr Banks expressed the view that the disclosure (by Mr A) and subsequent
management by both intern and supervisor represented
a departure
Significantly Below’
the reasonable standard and
would invite ‘
Severe Criticism’
(pg 7 of 18).

the mandatory requirements of the
Children and Young Person’s (Care and

(NSW) with respect to the children of Mr A.

“both intern and supervisor draw heavily on the ethical

” (pg 8 of 18). Dr

Profoundly Below

his summation Dr Banks included the following salient clinical observations at

From the notes available, I believe there was more than
sufficient information to know that it was well beyond this intern’s
of competence to be managing, much less leave her on her own to then expand her
mandate to include the children.
For a supervisor to argue that an intern was the most appropriate person
to intervene on this matter in light of the incremental but
ever-increasing
disclosures of abuse, and subsequent outcome highlights the inappropriate
management of this case from its earliest
The failure of two mandated reporters to fulfil their lawful obligations
to report this matter highlights a lack of competent supervision
and training in
key legislation that all
psychologists are not just expected to be
mindful of, but obligated to comply with. At the point of Ms Dene’s
inappropriate
disclosure during therapy of her own abuse history, it highlighted
yet another critical point when her supervisor should have stepped
terminate this process.
He (Dr Donnelly) encouraged the intern to continue with the case clearly
beyond her expertise, and due to her own abuse history, potentially
damaging not
just to client, and children, but the intern as well. He then referred the
children for ‘psycho-educational sessions’
to a well-trained, but
sadly, uninformed colleague whose intervention may have had a further negative
impact on the child he should
have been more focused on protecting.
He left his intern continuing to provide therapy, beyond a purportedly
completed practicum, and largely unsupervised, then to undertake
joint therapy
with both offender and victim, - while the victim still remained in an unchanged
Subsequent to his departure, and presumable on the basis of the inadequate
training and standards he espoused and/or provided his
intern, Ms Dene then
recklessly continued to provide therapy to this client, while not a student on
placement, but still not registered
as a psychologist and certainly not
supervised – but still not reporting further disclosures of abuse of the
subject children.

Banks clarified ‘child at risk of harm’ as follows (T55):
Q. I wonder whether you could explain to the tribunal what is
meant by "child at risk of harm" in your understanding as a
psychologist?
A. Two issues are meant, to me: firstly, that there is a harmful event
that has been identified, that an environment of clearly
less than optimal care
for the child has been provided, or further up that continuum, an environment
that has directly compromised
that child's safety and welfare, or wellbeing.
That's step one. The second is "at risk of harm" - the "at risk" element then
the likelihood - sorry, introduces the concept of likelihood: is
that same environment that produced the compromise to the welfare
of the child
likely to occur, or recur. So to me there are those two elements that need to
be satisfied.
Q. If I asked you to turn to the definition, as you've identified, there
is two elements to it. The first is, in the chapeau which
says, "A child or a
young person is at risk if current concerns exist for the safety, welfare or
wellbeing of the child." In your
opinion, would those concerns need to relate
to the child's physical wellbeing or could they encompass other aspects of the
A. I believe that it refers to - and when you consider (a) to (e) in
their totality, it considers the whole listed care of the child,
the safety,
welfare and wellbeing. As a clinician I believe they are all-encompassing
constructs.

Banks provided an explanation of the term ‘grooming’:
Q. And in your report, page 9 of 11 June 2009 report
relating to Ms Dene, you refer to an expression, "grooming", could you
explain what grooming is?
A. Grooming implies a pattern of behaviour seeking to alter the
individual, if I can engage the terms, perpetrator and victim, the
perpetrator
is seeking to change the victim's mindset and thought set about the activities
being engaged. There is a, if you like,
a dissonance, a disparity within the
victim's thoughts about what they think may or may not be correct behaviour
versus, if you like,
an instinctual or more primal protective basis of
behaviour. And you end up with a feeling of discomfort, uncomfortableness, and
fear. The grooming process is designed to alleviate, minimise, reduce that
discomfort such that the victim becomes more accommodating
as the process
continues. So if you like, the victim is started to be manipulated or swayed
over to the perpetrator's line of thinking
and pattern of behaviour.
Q. Well I asked you about grooming and the explanation you've given
relates very much to a grooming process between the perpetrator
and the victim,
but that doesn't appear to be the context in which you're using the phrase,
"grooming", in this report. Could you
explain the concept of grooming as
regards a psychologist which is the context in which it's used here?
A. I disagree, if I made reference to the exchange of emails between
Ms Dene and Mr A immediately after the events of 21 October,
you will note if you like a heavy emotional overlay between Mr A and
Ms Dene in the sense of him expressing remorse about the burden
that he had
placed upon her, wanting her, then further within that email wanting her to if
you like engage greater sympathy for him
in his predicament, I would argue that
he is placing additional pressure or burden on her to shift her thought
processes towards
him in a more - in a stronger allegiance.
Q. And that grooming process is that something of which you would have
expected a reasonable psychology intern with the training
and experience of
Ms Dene to be aware of?

Submissions - HCCC re Ms Dene

made a submission that invited the Tribunal to deal

of the Act. The HCCC stated that the
evidence of Ms Zandberg and the nature of the evidence and demeanour of Ms Dene
should be relied
upon in this regard.
9 to 11 inclusive set out the HCCC submissions regarding the obligations of an
intern psychologist. The Tribunal accepts
these submissions as accurately
setting out the obligations placed upon a provisionally registered psychologist
in the position of
Ms Dene and notes the submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene
does not directly address this matter.
HCCC submitted (at paragraphs 12 and 13) that given the definitions of
unsatisfactory professional conduct (section 25 of the
Act) and professional
misconduct (section 24 of the Act) Ms Dene’s own personal history and her
psychological or emotional
issues are not relevant considerations to the
characterisation of the conduct. The Tribunal accepts this submission as a
interpretation of the legislative test. Personal history, psychological
or emotional issues may well be matters that are relevant
to consideration of
the appropriate orders to be made by a Tribunal.
HCCC submitted that Ms Dene was providing health care or welfare services to
Miss B (at paragraph 14 of the submission) from October
2003 to August 2005. The
Tribunal accepts the evidence set out by the HCCC as supporting this submission,
commenting that the Tribunal
does not consider the question of whether it was Dr
Donnelly or Ms Dene that made the referral to Dr Brechman-Toussaint is of any
moment in the circumstances of this case. They were both actively involved in
the process.
15 to 19 of the HCCC submissions deal with the question of reporting obligations
of Ms Dene. The Tribunal accepts the submissions
made therein but does not rely
upon the payment of Ms Dene as the foundation for finding Ms Dene had an
obligation to report the
disclosure.
20 to 27 inclusive of the HCCC submissions relate to the nature and extent of
the disclosure made by Mr A in October 2003.
The Tribunal accepts the
submissions as an accurate representation of the nature of the disclosure made
by Mr A. The Tribunal further
observes that Ms Dene gave evidence that early in
the session on 21 October 2003 Mr A had told he was apprehensive about raising
matter with her because the topic was ‘taboo’. Disclosure of abuse
is also consistent with Ms Dene’s extreme
emotional reaction, her
inappropriate disclosure and the evidence regarding the discussions that took
place between Ms Dene and Dr
Donnelly regarding whether or not Ms Dene was a
mandated reporter.
HCCC made submissions regarding Ms Dene’s demeanour and manner at
paragraphs 28 to 39. The Tribunal accepts this description
of Ms Dene’s
conduct and demeanour.
reproducing Particular 1 relating to the Complaint against Dr Donnelly in the
submissions regarding Ms Dene (see page 14 of
the submissions), the content of
the submissions made from paragraphs 42 to 77 inclusive deal with the
Particulars of the Complaint
made regarding Ms Dene.
78 to 81 deal with the characterisation of the conduct of Ms Dene. The HCCC
submit that the Tribunal should make a finding
of professional misconduct.
HCCC submitted that it would be appropriate for the Tribunal to make a
Prohibition Order with respect to psychological services,
mental health
services, counselling and welfare services. The HCCC also submitted that a costs
order would be appropriate.
Submissions on behalf of Ms Dene
lengthy written submission on behalf of Ms Dene dated 16 October 2009 was
received from Unsworth Legal (Annexure C hereto). The
Tribunal has had regard to
the entirety of the submission. Despite the admission made on behalf of Ms Dene,
many of the Particulars
are denied in the submissions. Further, in many regards
the submissions do not sit with the Complaint as it came before the Tribunal
do they fit with the evidence given by Ms Dene. The Tribunal does not intend
dealing with every aspect of the submissions in
this document. The Tribunal sets
out in the following paragraphs some of the submissions made and makes comments
Tribunal is uncertain as to the intention of the submission setting out the
terms of section 34 of the Act. This section applies
to the processes that occur
prior to the referral of a matter to the Tribunal. As the section states, it is
the Board that confers
with the HCCC not the Tribunal. The Tribunal does not
‘confer’ with the HCCC. Rather, the Tribunal determines complaints
referred to it by the HCCC. The Tribunal has disregarded this submission as
irrelevant to the issues to be determined.
Tribunal accepts the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene at paragraph 9 as to
the application of the
‘Briginshaw’
test and is of the view
this test could be described as the
‘requisite standard of the balance
of probabilities’
referred to in paragraph 3 of the HCCC
submissions.
Tribunal notes the submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene regarding the
interpretation of section 24 of the Act (made in paragraphs
10 -13 of the
submissions). In so far as they accord with the Act, the Tribunal accepts the
submissions. The Tribunal notes that
the submissions do not make reference to
consideration of the psychologists
‘experience’
required by the Act. Further, the submissions appear to totally ignore the
provision that allows the Tribunal to making a finding
of unsatisfactory
professional conduct if reasonably satisfied that the conduct was
‘unethical’
. These are
matters that not only form part of section 24 of the Act but were clearly set
out in the Complaint and the evidence before
the Tribunal.
paragraph 14 a number of factors are listed as indicating that the admitted
conduct of Ms Dene does not fall ‘significantly
below’ that which is
‘reasonably expected of a psychologist of an equivalent level of
training or (sic) experience’
. The first matter raised is the
‘specific training’ of Ms Dene. The submissions note that Ms Dene
‘had not undertaken a professional clinical psychology session with any
prior to commencing her Degree in Clinical Psychology at the
University. The Tribunal accepts this is so but notes the significant
experience of Ms Dene in the related areas of school teaching, Family and Child
Mediation, Family and Child Counsellor and
Chairperson of Legal Aid Family Law
Conferencing. Even without such experience, the Tribunal expects that any
provisionally registered
psychologist will comply with legislative requirements,
including the Code of Conduct published by the Board. There can be no acceptance
of a position that would allow a registered health professional not to be
expected to comply with legislative requirements.
second example submitted as relevant to the question of the conduct failing to
reach the level of ‘significantly below’
is the fact that Ms Dene
was undertaking a practicum at the University at the relevant time. The Tribunal
notes that all provisionally
registered psychologists utilising the university
pathway to complete their hours of supervised practise will be completing a
that is supervised by a university. Ms Dene was in no different a
position to any other provisionally registered psychologists undertaking
graduate clinical studies.
submissions note that Ms Dene was supervised by two senior psychologists, one of
whom was responsible for teaching the child psychology
course at the University.
The Tribunal notes the evidence supports this submission and has had regard to
this matter in making the
findings set out below. Supervision, even supervision
by senior psychologists, does not negate personal professional responsibility.
Registration is a privilege which is granted to an individual who must then
ensure they practise to the appropriate standard. Given
the factual matrix of
this particular matter, responsibility cannot be abrogated by Ms Dene to the
degree submitted.
it was submitted on behalf of Ms Dene that the Tribunal would not find her
conduct ‘significantly below’ the
reasonable standard due to her
status as a
‘conditional’
registration status as opposed to
full registration. The Tribunal is of the view that matters such as compliance
with statutory obligations
and Codes of Conduct are essential for anyone holding
registration, no matter the class or nature of that registration. The Act does
not provide for a two tiered system of compliance with statutory obligations.
While it is reasonable to expect that a provisionally
registered psychologist
was developing clinical skills, all psychologists are expected to be practising
in accordance with statutory
requirements, all are expected to be practising
ethically, all are expected to be practising in a proper manner.
18 to 26 of the submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene deal with Particular 1(a).
The submission omits
‘and professional’
from the wording of
this Particular. This is a significant omission. Although the Tribunal is of the
view that there is a professional
obligation to practise in accordance with
legal obligations and professional obligations. Professional obligations are far
than legal obligations as it includes matters such as ethical and moral
considerations.
written submission states that Ms Dene does not admit Particular 1(a).
the context of the submission dealing only with the legal obligation of Ms Dene
and not her professional obligations, the Tribunal
notes the evidence that Ms
Dene did ultimately form the view that the conduct of Mr A should be reported to
DoCS. Ms Dene then set
herself up in a private practice, ‘prepared’
Mr A for the disclosure to DoCS and ultimately made a disclosure many months
Tribunal notes the submission made at paragraph 20 of Ms Dene’s
submissions to the effect that Ms Dene was able to rely
upon the views of her
two supervising psychologists. The Tribunal is of the view that it does not have
to deal with this submission
directly given the finding made below; however, the
Tribunal expresses the view that such a submission should be rejected. All
practitioners with registration have an obligation to ensure they practise
within professional boundaries. It is an obligation that
is not able to be
delegated. Decisions such as
HCCC v Nicholls
(Nurses’ and Midwives
Tribunal NSW, 26 June 2000) support the Tribunals view. While such
considerations may provide an explanation
for behaviour they do not excuse
behaviour. It would therefore be a matter to be properly considered by a
Tribunal when making protective
Tribunal has elsewhere dealt with the correct legal interpretation of
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act 1998
the Tribunal’s view, it is not necessary to consider in any detail the
submissions regarding whether or not Ms Dene
was providing services as part of
her ‘paid work’. While there was evidence of payments being made for
the work of Ms
Dene as part of the dementia care network, quite clearly, Ms Dene
was obtaining a benefit from her work as it was a requirement of
receiving her
final qualification and of having her provisional status removed from her
registration. The Tribunal reinforces its
view that the question of whether
monetary payment is made for services is not in any way relevant to the
professional obligations
of a psychologist.
Tribunal rejects the submission that Ms Dene was not providing
‘services to children’
. Going no further than the evidence
(e.g. the clinical notes and the evidence of Ms Dene) regarding the session
involving Miss B
such a submission must be rejected as without foundation. The
Tribunal does not accept that the evidence of Dr Banks supports the
that Ms Dene was not providing services to children.
submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene deal with Particular 1 (b) in paragraphs
27 to 30 inclusive. The submission states that
Ms Dene does not admit this
Particular.
Tribunal rejects the submission that there was no requirement under
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act 1998
a report for the reasons set out elsewhere in this document.
submission to the effect that the credit for the (ultimate) notification should
be attributed to Ms Dene is another matter the
Tribunal considers relevant to
protective orders. The weight given to this factor will be significantly
influenced by the delay in
the making of the report.
to the submission made in paragraph 29, there is no evidence before the Tribunal
to support the assertion that Dr Brechman-Toussaint
was in any way involved in
the care of Mr A.

Tribunal notes it was the Code of Ethical Conduct dated 3 November 1997 that was
in place at the time of the 21 October 2003 disclosure
by Mr A. This Code was
replaced by the Board with the Code of Professional Conduct dated 1 November
2004 (which became widely available
when a copy was provided in June 2005 to all
registered psychologists in NSW).
C4 of the 1997 Code imposes an obligation on registered psychologists to
communicate with the appropriate professionals
‘about the risks to the
welfare of people....such as children...’. Given the Tribunals finding of
fact regarding the
nature of the disclosure made by Mr A on 21 October 2003
there can be no doubt that the appropriate professionals for Ms Dene to
communicate with were officers from DoCS. While communication with supervisors
would be an appropriate step, communication with a
health professional within
the child protection framework is what is required by the Code of Ethical
39 and 40 of the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene relate to Particular 1(g).

ultimately made. The submission made at paragraph 40 indicates that Ms Dene

41 to 49 of the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene relate to Particular 1(h).
Ms Dene admits this Particular.
The Tribunal accepts the submission that Ms Dene was not told that she must
continue to treat Mr A but that she was advised that
she was suitable to
continue. The Tribunal accepts the evidence of Ms Boland (T18.6.10, page
That's a huge emotional message in that. And this man as a
supervisor has power. She does not want to disappoint him; she's going
guidance from him; and, she is going to in many cases override her own cognitive
and emotional responses, whatever they were,
to actually work with this client.
She's been put in a place - in a position of enormous dissonance.
accepting this position, the Tribunal also notes that Ms Dene did not take steps
to ensure that her notes were being reviewed by
Dr Donnelly. At this point the
Tribunal notes it does not accept Ms Dene’s evidence that her clinical
notes were not an accurate
record of what she was told by Mr A, rather they were
versions tainted by her own emotional state
. While it is certainly an obligation for a supervisor to
review the notes of the supervisee (a matter dealt with elsewhere in this
decision), in the circumstances of this matter, where Ms Dene was relying solely
on the views of her supervisors then it would be
incumbent upon her to ensure
they were provided with a totally accurate account of the sessions, undertake
her own independent consideration
of the matter, challenge the advice provided
by the supervisors and seek advice further afield.
there is strong criticism made by this Tribunal of Dr Donnelly’s role in
Ms Dene continuing to treat of Mr A, Ms Dene
must be held to be responsible for
her own conduct.

the evidence supports the assertion that Dr Donnelly advised Ms Dene to continue

professional competence with Dr Donnelly (or

registered psychologist’s responsibility to ensure they are
practicing within their field of competency. This principle is
applied across
all health professions.

by her willingness to raise these issues in

evidence of Ms Dene confirmed that the disclosure was spontaneous. In the view
of the Tribunal, it could not be characterised
as a ‘reflective
response’. Having already rejected Ms Dene’s evidence that her
records of the sessions were in
some way exaggerated by her own emotions
‘bleeding through’
the Tribunal accepts that Ms Dene most
likely was shocked and disturbed by the disclosure made by Mr A. A reaction in
the nature of
that of Ms Dene would not have been an expected professional
response of a registered psychologist. Even taking into account the
fact that Ms
Dene had limited experience working as a psychologist she had significantly more
experience than most provisionally
registered psychologists dealing with
children and family related issues. Ms Boland talks of
‘self-monitoring’ and reflection
on the part of Ms Dene. The
evidence is that Ms Dene immediately sought out Dr Donnelly and was so obviously
emotionally distressed
there can be no inference that there had been a conscious
decision on her part to raise the issue with her supervisor ‘the

that is before this current Tribunal, however, the

respect to Particular 3, the Tribunal notes that Ms Dene did not provide a copy

61 of the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene states that she relies on the

clinical notes reveal the rationale discussed between Ms Dene and Mr A for the

Findings – Generally
Tribunal has not had regard to the ultimate discovery of the extent of Mr
A’s paedophilic activity. As noted above, the
Tribunal characterises the
conduct having regard to the potential for harm rather than the actual harm that
eventuates. The ultimate
disclosure of Mr A’s paedophilic behaviour serves
as a reminder as to why the Parliament has made laws in this area and why
profession of psychology has developed a Code of Conduct, but nothing more.
it is clear that the evidence for the Complaint against each Respondent was
heard together the Tribunal has taken care to consider
the Complaint relating to
each of the Respondents separately and distinctly.
matter that may appear to cloud the consideration of this complaint is the
manner in which Mr A became a client. It is the
view of this Tribunal that the
manner in which Mr A came into the care of Ms Dene is ultimately of no
relevance. The fact is Mr A
became a client and Ms Dene provided therapy to him.
Further, when Ms Dene conducted a session with Miss B, she entered into a
professional
relationship with her. As to the relationship between Dr Donnelly
and Mr A, although it could be viewed as an intervention on the
supervisor in a supervisee’s session, there is evidence before the
Tribunal that Dr Donnelly’s involvement
was not superficial in nature. In
paragraph 80 of his statement (exhibit R14) Dr Donnelly stated that after Mr A
made the disclosure
to Ms Dene he ‘immediately arranged to meet with [Mr
A] and Ms Dene to investigate further to determine if it met the threshold
mandatory reporting’. Paragraph 81 of his statement provides details of
this meeting as follows:
The behaviour was described by Ms Dene and again by
as occurring approximately once per week for two years.
massage behaviour had stopped and
denied that there were any other
behaviours, past or present, suggesting abuse or neglect.

Tribunal notes the submissions made with respect to the scope of section 27
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act 1998
. As at 21 October
27 Mandatory reporting
(1) This section applies to:
(a) a person who, in the course of his or her professional work or other
paid employment delivers health care, welfare, education,
services, residential services, or law enforcement, wholly or partly, to
children, and
(b) a person who holds a management position in an organisation the duties
of which include direct responsibility for, or direct supervision
provision of health care, welfare, education, children’s services,
residential services, or law enforcement, wholly
or partly, to children.
(a) a person to whom this section applies has reasonable grounds to
suspect that a child is at risk of harm, and
(b) those grounds arise during the course of or from the person’s
the person must, as soon as practicable, report to the Director-General
the name, or a description, of the child and the grounds for
suspecting that the
child is at risk of harm.
Maximum penalty: 200 penalty units.
Sub-section
27(3) was added on to this section on 27 November 2003, it has no relevance to
the current matter.
was submitted on behalf of Ms Dene that the Tribunal should interpret
in the course of his or her professional work or other paid
employment...
’ to be read so that the section only applies to
professional work for which a person is paid
Following this
interpretation would result in a registered psychologist being a mandatory
reporter if his or her work was being renumerated
but not if it was being
undertaken on a pro bono basis. For the reasons set out below, the Tribunal
rejects the interpretation urged
by those acting for Ms Dene.
applying simple statutory interpretation principles there is nothing in the
construction of the sub-section that would require
to be read to be inserted before the words
‘professional work’
the interpretation urged upon the Tribunal would be inconsistent with the
general legislative framework of the
Children and Young Persons (Care and
Protection) Act 1998
it would be absurd for a registered psychologist to be a mandatory reporter if
they were being renumerated but not if it
was being undertaken on a
basis if presented with identical information regarding the welfare of
a child. Such a position has no place in the practise of the
profession of
psychology or any other profession. A member of the public is entitled to the
same protections and standards of care
from a health professional whether or not
that health professional is being renumerated.
the interpretation urged upon the Tribunal would be inconsistent with the
general ethical standards that registered psychologists
are required to adhere
to by virtue of their holding the privilege of registration.
the weight of the above the Tribunal does not consider further supporting
reasons are required. The Tribunal does however note
that the view of the
Tribunal coincides with the interpretation set out in a recent publication
The Structured Decision Making System – New South Wales
Mandatory Reporter Guide’
published on 24 November 2009 by the
Children’s Research Centre (www.nccd-crc.org). On page 103 of this
publication, a mandatory
reporter as defined by
‘....include those who deliver the following services wholly or partly
to children as part of their paid or professional work’.
‘Health Care’
is included in the
referred to. The same publication notes that the
legislation also
‘...mandates any person who manages an employee from
the above services to report risk of significant harm’.
Tribunal notes the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene regarding the fact that
she held ‘conditional’ rather than
full registration (paragraph 14,
submissions 16 October 2009). Once registration is granted, the Code of Conduct
applies. While the
experience of a practitioner is relevant to a consideration
of whether conduct amounts to unsatisfactory professional conduct, experience
does not excuse a departure from the Code. Relevantly, section 19(1) of the Act
clearly applies to all registered psychologists and
19 Code of professional conduct
(1) The Board may establish a code of professional conduct setting out
guidelines that should be observed by registered psychologists
professional practice. The Board may from time to time amend or replace a code
of professional conduct.

the first part of Dr Donnelly’s evidence set out above it is clear to the

the second part of the evidence of Dr Donnelly set out above, the Tribunal is

ordinarily be indicative of

response of Ms Dene to the disclosure, the content of the clinic notes

in October 2003 Dr Donnelly was aware that Mr A was disturbed about some aspect

pubic region and that the disclosure by Mr A had provoked Ms Dene to disclose

sexual abuse. He only realised that he may have been

behaviour was when giving evidence before the Tribunal. It was not a

Findings re Particulars - Ms Dene
respect to the HCCC submission inviting the Tribunal to make an additional
Complaint of impairment in relation to Ms Dene the
Tribunal accepts that it has
the power, in accordance with Clause 5(2) of Schedule 5 of the Act, to make
additional Complaints. The
Tribunal also accepts the HCCC submission that the
Tribunal would have an obligation to be ‘just and equitable’ in the
circumstances, although the Tribunal would take this further and state that if
any such steps were taken the principles of procedural
fairness would be
applied. The Tribunal has carefully weighed up this submission and considers it
is a matter for any subsequent
Tribunal that hears an application from Ms Dene
to be restored to the Register.
Dene accepted that there was a relationship between her own history of abuse and
the emotional reaction she experienced during
the session with Mr A on 21
October 2003. Only Ms Dene could know the full impact that this past history had
on her ability to work
in a professional fashion. Given the nature of the
disclosure made, her dramatic reaction to the situation, her limited experience
with this area of psychology she should have been able to form a view about her
fitness to continue with Mr A.
to the submissions made regarding Ms Dene’s demeanor, the Tribunal accepts
the submissions made by the HCCC (at paragraphs
28 to 30). The length of time
this matter has been under investigation and the stress caused by a hearing,
even remaining under cross-examination
for a lengthy period of time, is not
unusual in this, or any other jurisdiction.
respect to the parties’ submissions on the question of the candour and
insight of Ms Dene, the Tribunal accepts the submissions
made by the HCCC (dated
18 September 2009) at paragraphs 31 to 39. The HCCC submission accords with the
factual findings made elsewhere
in this document by the Tribunal.
respect to the submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene at paragraphs 116 to 120
the Tribunal cannot have regard to whether or not
Dr Donnelly and Ms Dene
‘agreed’ that Ms Dene would continue with the treatment after
September 2003 when she had completed
the hours required for that particular
practica. The reality was that Ms Dene commenced the treatment of Mr A as part
of the work
she was required to do to satisfy the university and she continued.
Professionally she had taken the responsibility for the care
Particular 1
the reasons set out above, the Tribunal is satisfied that on 21 October 2003 Mr
A disclosed to Ms Dene that he had touched his
daughter, Miss B, in an
inappropriate fashion for a period of over two years, having ceased that
behaviour only two weeks earlier.
respect to Particular 1(a), the Tribunal accepts the submissions made by the
HCCC from paragraph 44 to 48 and finds Ms Dene failed
to properly understand the
legal and professional obligations in cases of potential child abuse. The
Tribunal is of view this conduct
demonstrates a lack of knowledge, skill,
judgment and care in the practice of psychology that is significantly below the
reasonably expected of a psychologist of an equivalent level of
training or experience.
Tribunal has set out elsewhere in this document the correct interpretation of
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act
. The Tribunal is reasonably satisfied that Ms Dene failed to make a
report to DoCS as required by
when potential child abuse was
disclosed to her on 21 October 2003 (Particular 1 (b)). The Tribunal is of view
this conduct demonstrates
a lack of knowledge, skill, judgment and care in the
practice of psychology that is significantly below the standard reasonably
of a psychologist of an equivalent level of training or experience. The
Tribunal is also of the view that the conduct is unethical
and improper.
submissions made on behalf of Ms Dene do not appreciate the issues raised by
Particular 1(c) in terms of the practise of psychology.
It was Ms Dene who was
having the regular contact with Mr A. After Mr A made the disclosure on 21
October 2003 it was for her to
take steps to properly assess the risk to the
children. A disclosure of the nature made by Mr A is a
prima facie
indication that Miss B was at risk. There is nothing in the evidence to suggest
that Ms Dene took any steps to illicit further information
from Mr A regarding
his conduct. There is no evidence that Ms Dene looked to the vast amounts of
authoritative written material on
this topic for assistance in the assessment of
the information she was receiving from Mr A. Further, the contact between Ms
and Miss B was focused on Mr A’s needs rather than any fact finding
from Miss B (not that this would necessarily have been
appropriate in the
circumstances). The clinical notes made by Ms Dene strongly focus, to the point
of near total obsession, on the
needs of Mr A with respect to his inappropriate
conduct rather than there being a proper assessment or appreciation of the risk
the welfare of the children. If Ms Dene was to rely in any way on the input
of Dr Donnelly or Dr Brechman-Toussaint it was incumbent
upon her to have shown
each of them detailed notes regarding her sessions with Mr A. Clear
documentation of the information given
to both of them as well as the advice
provided should have been created by Ms Dene. The advice given would need to be
in unequivocal
terms and would then have to accord with Ms Dene’s own
assessment of the situation. The Tribunal accepts the submissions made
HCCC at paragraphs 49 to 52 of the HCCC submissions with respect to this
Particular and Particular 1(d). The Tribunal is reasonably
satisfied that Ms
Dene did not take adequate steps to assess whether or not Miss B or Miss C were
at risk and that this conduct demonstrates
a lack of knowledge, skill, judgment
and care in the practice of psychology that is significantly below the standard
reasonably expected
of a psychologist of an equivalent level of training or
