respect to Particular 3(c) the Tribunal repeats the view that the statutory
obligations placed on a registered psychologist cannot
be delegated to another
person. Even if it were the case that both Dr Brechman-Toussaint and Dr Donnelly
were fully informed of all
the information disclosed by Mr A and advised Ms Dene
that a report was not required, it was still incumbent on Ms Dene to satisfy
herself that this was in fact the case. While this may appear to be a high
standard, the Tribunal is of the view that this benchmarks
the appropriate
standard for a registered health professional. This is particularly so having
regard to the carefully crafted legislative
scheme that has been designed by the
Parliament to ensure that the protection of children from abuse is a paramount
consideration.
The evidence is that Dr Donnelly did not read the notes made by

not warranting such a report. The
Tribunal is of the view that this conduct demonstrates a lack of knowledge,
skill, judgment and
care in the practice of psychology that is significantly
below the standard reasonably expected of a psychologist of an equivalent
of training or experience. The Tribunal is also of the view that the conduct is
unethical and improper.

for a few more sessions to be held for a desired outcome to be

from incompetent and/or inexperienced treatment.
the comments made by the Tribunal in the findings regarding Particular 1(h),

into the clinical notes to the point that rendered the

respect to Particular 5(i) the Tribunal finds the evidence supports Ms
Dene’s admission of this Particular. The Code of
Ethical Conduct requires
that a psychologist only work within their area of competence. The additional
disclosures should have caused
Ms Dene to re-assess her own ability to deal with
the continuing care of Mr A. While it is to be expected that all psychologists
should be able to make appropriate initial assessments it is not expected that
all psychologists will be able to treat the full range
of clients. Not only have
clinical psychologists completed additional training in the treatment of
clients, specialist areas such
as Child and Adolescent Mental Health, Drug and
Alcohol Addiction and Sexual Assault have been well established within the
of psychology for many years. Ms Dene was the person with the primary
responsibility for the care of Mr A. Ms Dene had no specialised
training in this
area. The Tribunal finds that the conduct of Ms Dene is significantly below the
standard reasonably expected of
a psychologist of an equivalent level of
training or experience.

in the guidance provided by Dr Donnelly. The
input of Dr Brechman-Toussaint was
not ideal. The submission made on behalf of Ms Dene was to the effect that she
was placed in a
quite impossible situation
Dene’s submission - paragraph 128). Every registered health professional
has to ultimately take responsibility
for their own actions. That is an integral
part of the responsibility that accompanies the privilege of registration. The
before the Tribunal would not allow comfortable satisfaction that Ms
Dene revisited the situation with Dr Donnelly as new information
came to hand to
ensure she had the benefit of ‘
clarification, advice and
Tribunal accepts that Ms Dene’s conduct could be characterised as
misguided. To some extent it was misguided by the lack
of effective supervision;
however it was also misguided by factors internal to Ms Dene. Ms Dene did not in
any way make accommodation
for her reaction to the disclosure, for her own
history, for her limited experience in psychology, and in particular for her
of experience in this area of psychology and to the fact that Dr Donnelly
did not actively supervise her treatment of Mr A.
Tribunal has no doubt that Ms Dene thought she was acting with the best of
intentions in this matter. Whether or not conduct is
well intentioned is not the
test. In the matter of
Health Care Complaints Commission v Lopez
(30 September 2008) where the intention of the endorsed enrolled nurse
(EEN) was to administer medication to a patient. It was the
manner in which the
EEN went about administering the medication that was the issue, oral medication
via an IV line, that was the
misconduct.
Tribunal notes evidence given by Ms Dene herself confirms she was aware that
Miss B was being abused. The Tribunal looks to evidence
such as (T45.45-48,
Q. So you're clear that the content of what you were told was
A. Clearly, now when I look back, it was. I believed the child was
harmed. I didn't believe it was sexual harm.
the submission made on behalf of Ms Dene at paragraph 131 to the effect that if

she was entitled to receive
‘then the disclosure may have been made at a much earlier
is read with the word ‘
disclosure’
by the word ‘
’ meaning a report to DoCS then the
Tribunal accepts that it is likely that the report would have been made at an

Dene had formed the view for herself that the
conduct should be reported and at

Donnelly. There certainly
can be no offloading of Ms Dene’s responsibility
past the time she formed the view to report. Further, as noted elsewhere,
evidence of Ms Dene was that the advice she received was that she was not a
mandated reporter, rather than the conduct not being

Similarly, the submissions regarding the conduct of Ms Dene do not address the
issue of the period of time after

decision to report the conduct when making submissions regarding professional
misconduct.
to the submission at paragraph 134, the Tribunal does not find in the evidence
support for the submission that the peer reviewers
consider the conduct Ms Dene
acceptable. As set out elsewhere, while a provisionally registered psychologist
should look to their
supervisor for assistance and guidance there can be no
total abdication of professional responsibilities. At the end of the day it
the person in the treatment room with the client that bears the ultimate
responsibility for their conduct. This does not exculpate
a supervisor from
his/her own responsibilities as these exist separately and distinctly from the
responsibility of the supervisee.
Tribunal accepts the submissions made by the HCCC regarding the characterisation
of this conduct as professional misconduct (paragraphs

September 2009 submissions).
indicated above, the Tribunal finds the conduct of Ms Dene as set out in
Particular 4(c) constitutes professional misconduct. The

view that when the balance of the Particulars are considered together that the
conduct amounts to professional
misconduct in that the conduct was of a
sufficiently serious nature to justify suspension or cancellation of her
registration.
Protective Orders – Generally
primary function of the Tribunal is to protect the public. Other matters to be
considered by the Tribunal as relevant considerations
are the maintaining of
standards within the profession, a general deterrent effect and the maintaining
of public confidence in the
profession. The Tribunal therefore must consider
orders that fulfil these roles. Whilst it is an established principle of law
the Tribunal should impose the least restrictive Orders possible in the
circumstances, the protection of the public is paramount
and outweighs
consideration of the onerous burdens that Orders may place upon a practitioner.
In this matter the maintenance of confidence
that the public is entitled to
expect in the profession of psychology, the maintenance of proper standards
within the profession
and the general deterrent effect are prominent
considerations.
Protective Orders – Ms Dene
Tribunal accepts the submissions made by the HCCC in paragraphs 82 to 92 as to
the appropriate orders to be made. It is of significant
concern to the Tribunal
that Ms Dene did not have the insight to seek counselling until just recently,
and then it appears the arrangements
were made with the assistance of her legal
advisers. There is no doubt that Ms Dene requires treatment and it is
commendable that
her legal advisers have facilitated this clinical intervention.
Tribunal has carefully considered the role of the advice Ms Dene received from
her supervisors. It is indeed unfortunate that
Ms Dene was not more closely
supervised and was provided with supervision of such a poor quality. The
Tribunal accepts the HCCC submission
(made in reply dated 9 November 2009) that
Ms Dene is not entitled to rely on a claim that her conduct was subject to the
and guidance of her supervisor. The Tribunal accepts that at all times
Ms Dene had access to the DoCS helpline. Her apparent low
opinion of the
services provided by DoCS does not absolve her of acting in accordance with the
legal and professional requirements
of a registered psychologist.
conduct of Ms Dene was such that the Tribunal cannot be satisfied that Ms Dene
is currently safe to practise psychology even with
a comprehensive range of
conditions in place. The Tribunal accepts the submissions made by the HCCC
(particularly those set out in
paragraph 91 of the submissions dated 18
September 2009) as to the reasons why the only appropriate order to be made is
one of de-registration,
or if she is not currently on the Register and order
that she remain off the Register for a period of time.
Tribunal has carefully considered the appropriate time for Ms Dene to remain off
the Register. At least by 21 October 2003 Ms
Dene became aware that her own
history had intruded into her practice as a psychologist. Until just recently,
Ms Dene had not sought
any treatment in that regard, and then only did so with
the assistance of her legal advisers. The issues facing Ms Dene are well
entrenched. The evidence before the Tribunal supports there being no
‘quick fix’ for these issues. The nature of the
conduct is also
relevant. Ms Dene allowed children to remain in a highly vulnerable risk of harm
situation for a very significant
prolonged period of time. The Tribunal has had
regard to the references provided by two colleagues and a friend and has no
that Ms Dene performed well as a mediator.
was however nothing before the Tribunal, apart from comments from Ms Dene, which
would provide any reassurance that conduct
of this nature would not recur or
that Ms Dene’s own history would not intrude into her practice again. Time
to read and consider
ethical matters will certainly assist Ms Dene. Weighing all
factors relevant to the consideration of the minimum period Ms Dene should
remain off the register the Tribunal is of the view that a minimum period of
years is required.
Tribunal is reasonably satisfied that Ms Dene currently poses a substantial risk
to the safety of the public, particularly with
respect to any matters involving
sexual assault. The Tribunal will make a Prohibition Order with respect to the
provision of health
service that involve therapy, counselling, psychotherapy,
hypnotherapy, mental health services, any other alternative/healing
healthcare/therapies
and welfare service as defined in
Health Care Complaints Act 1993
the Respondent makes an application for re-registration, this Tribunal is of the
view that such an application should be determined
by a Tribunal.
While this decision makes it quite clear that a formal Complaint of impairment
was not considered by the Tribunal, the fragile mental
health of Ms Dene
certainly played a significant role in many aspects of this matter. The mental
health status of Ms Dene is certainly
a matter that should be considered if an
application for re-registration is made, or indeed if the matter is in any way
or considered.
Submissions of Ms Dene re Suppression Order and Costs
application for a suppression order with respect to the personal details of Ms
Dene in the final submissions is not granted. The
Tribunal notes the evidence
was given in an open hearing. The basis for the application does not meet the
‘special and exceptional circumstances
’ set by the
Court of Appeal in
Momot & Anor
NSWCA 17 April 1997.
Further, an understanding of the background of Ms Dene provides the context for
her reaction and is essential
to be an integral part of her addressing the
deficits in her practice demonstrated by the decision in this matter.
submissions did not provide any reasons why the costs in the matter should be
limited to four days. While the length of the hearing
was marginally increased
by the matter being heard together, the total hearing time for two separate
matters would have been higher.
As each Respondent is only bearing half of the
costs, the results will be lower than they would have been in a separate
Orders re Ms Dene
Tribunal orders:
(1) That the
name of the Respondent, Ms Dene, be cancelled from the Register from close of
business on 6 August 2010 (if her name
has not already been removed
administratively). From the date of this document until 6 August 2010 Ms Dene is
to limit her practise
(if she is entitled to practise) to current clients and is
to only see each client on no more than two occasions for the purpose
terminating treatment and referring the clients on appropriately.
(2) That The
Respondent, Ms Dene, is not to reapply for registration for a minimum period of
three years from the date hereof.
(3) That the
Respondent, Ms Dene, is prohibited from providing the following health services
for a period of three years from close
of business on 6 August 2010 (assuming
she holds current Registration) or from the date hereof if she does not):
(i) therapy
(ii) counselling
(iii) psychotherapy
(iv) hypnotherapy
health service
(vi) welfare
services (as defined in
Health Care Complaints Act 1993
(vii) any other
alternative/healing health care/therapies.
(4) After the expiry of the period of three years in Order 3 above, the
Respondent, Ms Dene, is permitted to provide these health
services on the
condition that she gains registration as a psychologist or other health
professional, at which time the registration
authority should consider the
further continuation of the prohibition order.
(5) The Respondent, Ms Dene, is to pay the costs of the HCCC with respect to the
preparation of her matter for hearing and half of
the HCCC costs with respect to
the joint hearing.

70 of the submissions made on behalf of Dr Donnelly address an
‘allegation that Dr Donnelly permitted Ms Dene to provide therapy to Mr
A over the course of at least 42 sessions as being excessive’
indicates this allegation is also disputed. This is the subject of Particular 8
of the Complaint. It is submitted that the involvement
of interns in long-term
treatment is a matter
‘on which different minds will
. Reference is made to the evidence of Dr Donnelly (T17/8/09,
41.49-42.43). This evidence was:
Q. Dr Donnelly, can I ask you, you used a reference in
giving your evidence a short time ago to long-term therapy, can you explain
you haven't already and tell me what if the evidence you have given covers this,
but why did you make the decision to involve
Ms Dene is what has been
described as long-term therapy?
A. The point of reference is in the literature. This idea of revolving
door therapy, there is a dissertation by Lenneberg in 1999
that speaks to it but
this is in Australia, it's a clinical training program in South Australia. But
again from my perspective with
my training to not expose interns to long-term
therapy is highly unethical. I think it throws people into the deep-end when
out there as a fully registered psychologist to have never seen a case
for more than eight weeks I think it's just unfathomable to
me. It was one of
the huge flaws that I saw in the training program. Now when you think about a
case that really requires long-term
treatment, for example, a family coping with
a mother with a chronic illness that's degenerative, where there are repeated
in function, repeated events that cause a destabilising effect within a
family, that there are new therapeutic issues, to think that
what you should do
is have someone see that case like in a university clinic or even a clinical
psychologist fully registered, to
change therapists every six months, every
year, for a 20-year course of treatment it's absolutely ludicrous. I think it's
good treatment.
The other side of it is that, how do you deal with transference and
countertransference issues for interns if you don't ever get to
see them. You
can't insulate interns from that kind of - from that experience and then expect
them to cope with it effectively down
the road. It sets them up to have the
reactions that are cited in the literature over and over, that's why it's
dangerous to not
deal with countertransference issues in supervision. The fact
that the experts have talked about here - I know Dr Banks has absolutely
experience with long-term therapy. He testified to that. He has no experience
in providing long-term supervision.
I think it's a foreign concept and it's foreign concept that's very well
accepted in Europe and very well accepted in the US that
it's the richness of
the experience is so much greater than what's available in the training programs
that I've been exposed to here
that I constantly battle and think about how can
I create some kind of situation where an intern can feel what it's like to have
a therapeutic relationship where there's stuff comes up and they actually get a
chance to work through it in supervision. So this
was a case that called for
long-term therapy. We specifically got university department approved funding,
a collaboration with the
division of general practice to create opportunities
for the interns so they could have exposure to long-term cases, dementing cases,
people with traumatic brain injuries, people with multiple sclerosis where we
knew it was going to be a long-term course.
submission also notes that Dr Donnelly provided clinical references that
supported the view that helping an intern to work through
countertransference
issues was an essential part of their training. The submission is critical of
the view of Dr Banks on the basis
that he provided no justification for his
statement other than the ‘outcome’ of the current matter and notes
‘does not advance any further basis as to why such
practice would be hazardous to intern and client’.

Submissions in reply – HCCC re Dr Donnelly

Tribunal’s Findings regarding Particulars of Complaint
- Dr Donnelly
Findings re the Reports of Dr Banks
setting out the Tribunal’s findings with respect to each of the
Particulars, the Tribunal will deal with the submissions
made on behalf of Dr
Donnelly regarding the evidence of Dr Banks.
Tribunal has set out above reasons for rejecting the position put forward by Ms
Dene that her clinical notes were tainted by a
‘bleeding through’ of
her own sexual abuse history. The Tribunal is of the view that Dr Banks is
entitled to rely on
the content of the notes. Further, the Tribunal accepts the
evidence of Dr Banks that he appreciated that the full extent of Mr A’s
history of sexual abuse was not known to Ms Dene or Dr Donnelly. Counsel for Ms
Dene squarely raised this issue while Dr Banks was
giving evidence and Dr Banks
indicated that his answers were either ‘generic’ in nature (e.g.
that psychological harm
flows from sexual abuse) or related to the information
he gathered from the clinical notes made by Ms Dene. Dr Banks is experienced
the requirements of an expert witness. Having stated elsewhere that the Tribunal
is mindful of the protective nature of this jurisdiction
and the potential for
harm rather than the actual harm, the Tribunal has considered Dr Banks’
evidence in this light.
Particular 1
made factual findings regarding the nature of the disclosure made by Mr A on 21
October 2003, the Tribunal notes the acceptance
of Dr Donnelly of findings with
respect to Particulars 1 (a), (b), (f) and (g). The Tribunal is of the view that
the evidence supports
the acceptance of Dr Donnelly of findings with respect to
these Particulars. The Tribunal is of view this conduct demonstrates a
knowledge, skill, judgment and care in the practice of psychology that is
significantly below the standard reasonably expected
of a psychologist of an
equivalent level of training or experience. The conduct in Particular 1(b) is
also found by the Tribunal
to be improper and unethical, and the conduct in 1(g)
to be unethical.
is unfortunate that Particular 1(g) was amended during the hearing. It is highly
desirable for there to be a correct reference
to the Code of Ethical Conduct.
However, the conduct complained of was clearly described in the Particular and
falls within Clause
C4 of the 1997 Code. While it is the view of the Tribunal
that the conduct is fundamentally of a serious nature, it is to be viewed
context of the conduct being a breach of the Code. The Code should have been
well known to Dr Donnelly, not just as it related
to his own practice but also
as it was part of his role as supervisor to monitor Ms Dene’s compliance
with the Code.
was the evidence of Ms Dene that Dr Donnelly was asked into the session with Mr
A shortly after 21 October 2003 because of Mr A’s
reaction to being
advised that Dr Donnelly had been informed of the disclosure that he had made to
Ms Dene on 21 October 2003 and
Mr A was concerned about confidentiality. While
the Tribunal is satisfied on the evidence of Ms Dene that the session involving
Donnelly was not planned or instigated by Dr Donnelly the Tribunal also notes
that there are no notes produced by Dr Donnelly with
respect to the session he
had with Mr A. Not only is it an expectation that a written record be kept of
any sessions undertaken by
a registered psychologist, if the purpose of the
session was as stated on behalf of Dr Donnelly it would have been an absolute
for a written record to have been made.
if the Tribunal accepted the submission made on behalf of Dr Donnelly as to the
nature of the disclosure reported to him as having
been made by Mr A, the
Tribunal is reasonably satisfied that a disclosure of this nature is sufficient
to raise prima facie concerns
for the welfare of Miss B.
is significant that the submission made on behalf of Dr Donnelly as to the
nature of the disclosure made by Mr A to Ms Dene omits
reference to the
disclosure being made in to the context of Mr A’s statement that the topic
he wished to raise being
nor the reaction that it
provoked in Ms Dene. Such context is significant and relevant to the proper
consideration of the situation.
Tribunal has considered the submissions made by Dr Donnelly regarding the use of
inappropriate
’ in the Particulars. The Tribunal
does not accept Ms Dene’s explanation that it was the
dependency’
of Mr A on his daughter that was described by
subject. There are numerous aspects of the
evidence that allow the Tribunal to be reasonably satisfied that this is so.
Significantly,
the Tribunal has the evidence of Ms Dene’s reaction to the
disclosure made by Mr A (described elsewhere) and the context of
the content of
the balance of the clinical notes.
extract of Dr Donnelly’s evidence relied upon (17.8.09, T29.44- 30.31
– see paragraph 40 of submissions) in support
of the submission that Mr
A’s lack of comfort surrounding the massage of Miss B related to a non
sexual boundary violation
is an example of many of the concerns the Tribunal
generally had with the evidence. Dr Donnelly’s evidence was distracted
references to the grave illness of Mrs A, overriding concern for the well being
of Mr A and distracting unsupported propositions.
With respect to this latter
issue the Tribunal notes that Dr Donnelly gave evidence as to the appropriate
use of massage ‘
in dealing with kids with psychosomatic
. While the Tribunal would not have difficulty accepting
massage as an appropriate tool to assist with all manner of ailments, there
no evidence before the Tribunal that Miss B was suffering from any
‘psychosomatic’ symptoms as alluded to in the
evidence of Dr
Donnelly. Further, appropriate massage, no matter what the symptoms, would
involve a hand being brushed or moved over
the pubic area of a child.
Tribunal notes that Dr Donnelly did not ever make a voluntary report to DoCS
despite signing the discharge summary in evidence.
The Tribunal is reasonably
satisfied that Particular 1 (c) is established. The Tribunal is of view this
conduct demonstrates a lack
of knowledge, skill, judgment and care in the
practice of psychology that is significantly below the standard reasonably
of a psychologist of an equivalent level of training or experience. The
Tribunal is also of the view that the conduct is unethical
and improper.
Donnelly also made lengthy and detailed submissions to the effect that he did
consider whether or not to make a report and then
reassessed whether or not to
make a report after further disclosures were made by Mr A (see T 18.8.09, pages
10.39 -11.30). As set
out elsewhere the Tribunal is of the view that Dr Donnelly
failed to take basic and fundamental steps to assess whether or not Miss
Miss C were at risk. The Tribunal is reasonably satisfied that Particular 1 (d)

the Tribunal is of the view that there were absolutely compelling reasons for

respect to Particulars 1(h) and (i), the Tribunal is of the view that having
knowledge of either Ms Dene’s limited experience
or her history of child
sex abuse, would form the basis for a psychologist of Dr Donnelly’s
training and experience to have
removed Ms Dene from providing any further
treatment services. Noting the submission made on behalf of Dr Donnelly
regarding the
assumption as to the nature of the disclosure that was made by Mr
A on 21 October 2003, the Tribunal has dealt with the nature of
the disclosure
made and rejects the submission that the disclosure would need to have been that
there was touching of Miss B’s
pubic area in a sexual way and that he had
sexual thoughts about his daughter was required before it was appropriate for a
to be made against Dr Donnelly with respect to these two Particulars.
Putting aside the question of the disclosure made by Ms Dene
to Mr A, in the
view of the Tribunal all that was required was the knowledge of touching of the
pubic area in the manner found by
the Tribunal to have been disclosed by Mr A.
The test does not involve a finding of what was in the mind of Mr A at the time
he undertook
the touching. The Tribunal emphasises the view that a disclosure of
the nature made by Ms Dene would inevitably compromise the future
therapeutic relationship between Mr A and Ms Dene. Such a situation requires
careful consideration on the part of a supervisor
and may involve the referral
of the supervisee for counselling/therapy. While a degree of counselling may be
properly part of a supervision
relationship, it is not appropriate for it to be
a focus of supervision.
Tribunal is of view this conduct demonstrates a lack of knowledge, skill,
judgment and care in the practice of psychology that
is significantly below the
standard reasonably expected of a psychologist of an equivalent level of
training or experience. The Tribunal
is also of the view that the conduct is
unethical and improper.

Particular 4
respect to Particular 4 the Tribunal notes that Ms Dene clarified the time that
entries of supervision ceased being recorded
in the log book as being the time
that her university practicum ended. Although Ms Dene’s evidence is not
entirely straightforward,
she does clearly describe supervision during the time
she was treating Mr A as ‘
the novelty of a long term client for Ms Dene, her reaction during the session
with Mr A on 21 October 2003 and what Dr Donnelly
described as the
complicated clinical presentation’
there was no scope for Dr
Donnelly to depart from a diligent supervision regime.
failure to record supervision (both the fact that supervision had occurred in a
log book format as well as a record of the substance
of the supervision) is a
significant departure from the standard of supervision expected of any
registered psychologist as it is
an integral part of supervision of the nature
undertaken by Dr Donnelly of Ms Dene. The process of reviewing clinical notes to
they are accurately and appropriately kept is also essential to
supervision. Failure to review the clinical notes is a more fundamental
departure from the standard reasonably expected of any registered
psychologist.
Tribunal also considers relevant to the finding with respect to this Particular
the following additional factors submitted by
That supervision
occurred by telephone for a period of approximately two months during 2004;
That Dr Donnelly
did not once read Ms Dene’s clinical records and only attended one session
with Mr A (being 27 October 2003).
conduct of Dr Donnelly in failing to appropriately supervise Ms Dene warrants
more than just a comment. Even if this was just
the first long term therapy that
had been provided by Ms Dene or if she had had the reaction described by Dr
Donnelly to the session
with Mr A on 21 October 2003, the circumstances would be
such that Dr Donnelly should not have departed from basic supervision standards
or as Dr Donnelly described as his ‘
own model of
supervision
supervision
’ provided by Dr
Donnelly on this occasion was extremely deficient in many respects and falls
significantly below the standard
reasonably expected of a psychologist of an
equivalent level of training and experience. The Tribunal is also of the view
conduct is unethical and improper. The Tribunal makes a finding of
unsatisfactory professional conduct. The Tribunal is of the view
failures with respect to this Particular was of a sufficiently serious nature to
justify suspension or cancellation of Registration.
The Tribunal makes a finding
of professional misconduct with respect to this particular.
exercising protective jurisdictions frequently frame criticism in terms of the
potential for harm of particular conduct.
This flows from the protective rather
than punitive nature of the jurisdiction. In this matter the potential for harm
have been, by and large, realised. This matter should act as a
reminder to practitioners who accept the responsibility of supervision
discharge their professional obligation with a high degree of diligence.

article of a

vulnerable.
are a number of aspects of the evidence before the Tribunal that would not allow

have read the clinical notes made by Ms Dene. In the event that the notes

is discernable from the findings in this matter, the Tribunal has not relied on

elsewhere rejected the submission that the clinical record written by Ms Dene
was not accurate due to her own issues
‘bleeding through’
The Tribunal accepts that the clinical records made by Ms Dene were in some
respects clinically poor, they record in many places
in a coherent and
consistent manner that Ms Dene was concerned with the conduct of Mr A towards
Tribunal has not relied upon the information not ‘known’ at the time
of the events particularised and that has subsequently
become known through the
disclosures made by Mr A to Cedar House. The Tribunal notes at this point that
the pattern of conduct displayed
by Mr A might be described as
‘textbook’. He made incremental disclosures of his conduct and he
recruited the support
and friendship of Ms Dene. An important aspect of the
practice of psychology is an awareness of the potentially manipulative nature
some clients. This does not appear to have been considered by Ms Dene, which may
be explained by her relative inexperience and
her own personal vulnerabilities
but cannot be explained away for a person in Dr Donnelly’s position.
matter what the circumstances of the signing, it is indeed disturbing that Dr
Donnelly signed the Discharge Summary for Mr A given
the content thereof without
making a report to DoCS. Indeed, if this was the only matter before the Tribunal
a finding of professional
misconduct would be made.
unacceptable aspect of this matter is the lack of record of there having been
any intervention with Mr A to deal with the
impact of the disclosure made by Ms
Dene on 21 October 2003. As it eventuated it became apparent that Mr A had quite
a deal of experience
of child sexual assault however for many clients a
disclosure of this nature by a psychologist would be at least distressing.
respect to the issues of Dr Donnelly’s candour and insight the Tribunal
accepts the submissions of the HCCC (paragraphs
27 to 37). The Tribunal makes
the following specific comments:
Not only did Dr
Donnelly seek to minimise the nature of the disclosure he became aware of in
October 2003, the version put before
the Tribunal was not consistent with the
advice Dr Donnelly had given to the University when they investigated this
matter, or with
the responses he provided to the HCCC or with the evidence of Dr
Brechman-Toussaint nor with the contents of Ms Dene’s notes.
With respect to
the HCCC’s submission at paragraph 34(b) the Tribunal is of the view that
the context in which the disclosure
was made by Mr A cannot be accepted as an
‘explanation’ when dealing with a registered psychologist with the
and experience of Dr Donnelly.
respect to the submissions made regarding the reporting obligations under
of the relevant Act the Tribunal has set out its views elsewhere in
this document.
the transcript references provided by Dr Donnelly to support a finding that he
had considered whether the circumstances warranted
the making of a report to
DoCS but had decided the circumstances did not warrant the making of a report,
the Tribunal is unable to
accept this position. The evidence before the Tribunal
is that Dr Donnelly did not read the clinical notes made by Ms Dene. Dr Donnelly
did not assert that he forensically questioned Ms Dene about the disclosure made
by Mr A, despite Ms Dene’s intense emotional
reaction during the session
on 21 October 2003. There are no records of the supervision sessions between Ms
Dene and Dr Donnelly
that may have assisted Dr Donnelly in this regard.
is the firm view of the Tribunal that having been told that Mr A was distressed
that he had committed a
‘boundary violation’
by giving a
massage to Miss B at night wherein his hand moved or brushed over her pubic area
it was incumbent upon Dr Donnelly to
undertake further investigations if he was
to appropriately consider the matter.
Findings in relation to the Complaints – Dr
Complaint One
set out above, the Tribunal is comfortably satisfied that the conduct set out in
each of the Particulars of Complaint constitutes
unsatisfactory professional
conduct. Complaint One is established.
Complaint Two
indicated above, the Tribunal finds the conduct of Dr Donnelly as set out in
Particular 4 constitutes professional misconduct.
The Tribunal is also of the
view that when the balance of the Particulars are considered together that the
conduct amounts to professional
misconduct in that the conduct was of a
sufficiently serious nature to justify suspension or cancellation of his
registration.
Protective Orders – Dr
that the Tribunal has reserved on the question of Prohibition Orders and sought
further submissions from the HCCC and Dr Donnelly
on this matter, the Tribunal
(1) That the
name of the Respondent, Dr Donnelly, be removed from the Register at close of
business on 6 August 2010. From the date
of this document until 6 August 2010 Dr
Donnelly is to limit his practise to current clients and is to only see each
client on no
more than two occasions for the purpose of terminating treatment
and referring the clients on appropriately.
(2) That the
Respondent, Dr Donnelly, is not to reapply for registration for a minimum period
of three years commencing from 7 August
Respondent, Dr Donnelly, is to pay the costs of the HCCC with respect to the
preparation of her matter for hearing and half
of the HCCC costs with respect to
the joint hearing.
General comment
stated above, this Tribunal does not consider the actual harm that results from
a health care professional’s action when
determining the appropriate
orders to be made once a finding of professional misconduct (or unsatisfactory
professional conduct)
is made. The Tribunal does however have a role in
providing guidance to the profession and for the protective orders made to
a deterrent effect to other members of the profession. Given the current
subject matter, the Tribunal notes that with respect to
the abuse of children,
harm accrues over time. It has been well understood and accepted for many years
by health professional such
as psychologists that more harm will result from
longer exposure to abuse. It is imperative that all registered psychologists
considered the mandatory reporting obligations.

copy of this document should be forwarded to each of the Respondents and the
respective legal advisers, the HCCC, Mr Dale Tolliday,
the Regional University
and otherwise as the Board sees fit.

HCCC v DENE & DONNELLY
HCCC SUBMISSIONS RE: DENE
Introduction
this matter the Health Care Complaints Commission (HCCC) makes two complaints as
regards the conduct of Ms Dene from around October
2003 until August 2005. Ms
Dene is a registered psychologist with a certificate of registration under
section 123
Psychologists Act 2001
(the Act) valid until 30 June
2010. From 2 April 2002 Ms Dene was conditionally registered as a psychologist,
attaining full registration
on 9 April 2005.
complaint comprises seven separate particulars. One complaint is of
unsatisfactory professional conduct under section 25 of the
Act, the second is
of professional misconduct under section 24 of the Act.
complaints should be found proved. The evidence clearly establishes, to the
requisite standard of the balance of probabilities,
that Ms Dene’s conduct
is unsatisfactory professional conduct within s 25 of the Act, in that the
knowledge, skill or judgment
possessed, or care exercised, by Ms Dene in her
practice of psychology was significantly below the standard reasonably expected
a psychologist of an equivalent level of training or experience and/or she
engaged in improper or unethical conduct in the course
of the practice or
purported practice of psychology, and this was of a sufficiently serious nature
to justify suspension or cancellation
of her registration as required by s 24 of
given the nature of the evidence and demeanour of Ms Dene (as set out below) and
the evidence of Ms Zandberg, the Tribunal
is invited, pursuant to the power set
out in paragraph 5(2) of Schedule 5 to the Act, to treat as referred to it and
deal with an
additional complaint that Ms Dene suffers from an impairment that
detrimentally affects or is likely detrimentally to affect her
mental capacity
to practise psychology, namely Post Traumatic Stress Disorder. The
Tribunal’s power in this regard is subject
to an obligation, prior to
dealing with the complaint, to give such adjournment (if any) as is, in the
opinion of the Tribunal, just
and equitable in the circumstances. Whilst that is
a matter upon which Ms Dene’s representatives may wish to make
submissions,
it may well be that the evidence is such that there would be no
requirement in this regard for any further evidence, and that further
submissions on this point from Ms Dene’s representatives could be
accommodated within the current timetable.
submissions set out below as to Orders are made on the basis of the complaints
currently before the Tribunal. If the Tribunal
adds and deals with a complaint
regarding impairment, it will remain the position of the Commission that the
Orders as set out below
are the appropriate orders.
Dene has a background in teaching and counselling services, including as regards
families. She completed a Bachelor of Psychology
with honours in 1999. She was
conditionally registered as a psychologist on 2 April 2002. She then undertook a
doctorate in psychology
between 2002 and 2004, but did not qualify at doctoral
level. She undertook a number of practica, two supervised by Dr Donnelly,
one supervised jointly by Dr Donnelly and Dr Brechman-Toussaint. By the end of
2004 Ms Dene had completed all of her training
and supervision.
Dene came to see Mr A initially as part of a practicum with Dr Donnelly, and
probably also as part of work which she completed
as an intern working under Dr
Donnelly’s supervision pursuant to a contract between the Regional
University and the New England
Division of General Practice for the provision of
neuropsychological and other related services. By September 2003 Ms Dene had
two practica, one with Dr Donnelly and one with Dr Brechman-Toussaint.
When Ms Dene completed her practicum with Dr Donnelly in September
2003, she and
Dr Donnelly agreed that she should continue treating Mr A as a long-term patient
under Dr Donnelly’s supervision,
albeit not formally under the structure
of an ongoing
her first long term clinical psychological interaction.
General Principles
number of issues of general application arise in respect of the
complaints.
Obligations of an intern
Ms Dene was at all times supervised by Dr Donnelly in her interactions with Mr
A, she was subject to professional and legal
obligations as a conditionally
registered psychologist. In this regard, she owed duties to the public, to Mr A,
and to Miss B and
set out in the [Regional University]
an intern was obliged to look critically at herself and her therapeutic role, to
recognise her own professional and personal
limitations, and to uphold
professional standards. Whilst she could certainly expect guidance from Dr
Donnelly as her supervisor,
she cannot abdicate her own responsibility by
reference to that expectation.
notwithstanding her status as an intern, Ms Dene was clearly personally
responsible for upholding the requirements of the
obligation to ensure that nothing of a psychological nature was done with or to
Miss B without her proper informed and voluntary
consent (A1 of 1997 Code). This
meant as regards Miss B that she should not have been made subject to the
treatment session on 15
September 2004 without the nature of that session having
been properly discussed with her before hand;
obligation to safeguard the welfare of Mr A and Miss B (A5 of 1997
obligation to refer for more specialised service those clients whose needs were
outside Ms Dene and/or Ms Dene as supervised by
Dr Donnelly’s,
professional competence (A8 of 1997 Code). The obligation to refer also includes
the obligation to ensure that
appropriate information is made on the referral so
that the referral is effective.
obligation to uphold the law (C1 of 1997 Code);
obligation to communicate to appropriate professionals about the risks to the
welfare of a child (C4 of 1997 Code).
obligation to maintain knowledge and practice at an acceptable level of
competence and to recognise the psychological issues involved
professional relationships, including transference and power differentials (D2
of 1997 Code). This would include both the
relationship between Dr Donnelly and
Ms Dene, and the relationship between Ms Dene and Mr A.
obligation to withdraw from practice and seek professional help, where
appropriate for impairment if she saw impairment of her
ability to practise (D4
of 1997 Code).
obligation to demonstrate continuing competence in her practice of psychology
that includes adequate knowledge, skill, judgement
and care (A1 of 2004
obligation to maximise benefit and do no harm in the practice of psychology (A2
of 2004 Code);
obligation to act ethically and properly and to promote accuracy, fairness and
honesty in their practice of psychology (A4 of
2004 Code);
obligations to give serious consideration to the potential vulnerability of
children and to refer, for more specialised service,
those clients whose needs
or requests are outside their professional competencies (B3 of 2004
duty to communicate information about serious concerns about the well-being or
safety of children to appropriate professionals
or organisations (B4 of 2004
obligation to be aware of relevant guidelines (C of 2004
Standard required of Ms Dene
the purposes of the definition of unsatisfactory professional conduct in s 25 of
the Act the standard of knowledge, skill and
judgment against which Ms Dene is
to be measured is that of “
a psychologist of an equivalent level of
training or experience
”. This standard is the basis of the definition
of professional misconduct in s 24 of the Act.
Dene’s own personal history and psychological or emotional issues are thus
not in any way relevant to the question of whether
or not the complaints in this
case are proved.
Obligations as regards Miss B
Ms Dene’s primary clinical interactions were with Mr A, it is clearly the
case that throughout the period from October
2003 up until August 2005 she was
also providing health care or welfare services to Miss B:
Dene’s paramount concern after the disclosure in October 2003 should have
been to protect Miss B’s
Dene’s therapeutic concern as regards the A family was with the family as
was a child related interaction in the context of family
all times from October 2003 Miss B’s welfare was part of the
considerations taken into account by Ms Dene in framing her
interactions, and her treatment goals, with Mr A;
Dene was in effect providing therapeutic services for Miss B in referring her to
Dr Brechman-Toussaint, and in discussing her situation
and treatment needs with
Dr Brechman-Toussaint;
reason of the disclosure of Mr A in October 2003 as regards Miss B her welfare
became a therapeutic concern of Ms Dene; and
Dene also undertook a direct clinical interaction with Miss B on 15 September
2004, and spent time planning for, and (with Mr
A) dealing with the
ramifications of, this session, including considering whether to conduct a
second session.
Reporting Obligations and
Children and Young Persons (Care and Protection) Act 1998
obligation under
Children and Young Persons (Care and
Protection) Act 1998
respectively) are clear. They require
that a report be made to the Director-General as soon as practicable where (so
far as is relevant
to the present case):
person in the course of her professional work delivers or supervises the
provision of health care or welfare services wholly or
partly to children. That
unquestionably covers Ms Dene as regards this particular case.
person has reasonable grounds, arising during the course of or from his work, to
suspect that a child is at risk of harm, meaning
concerns exist for the safety, welfare or well-being of the child; and
concerns are because of one or more of the child having been, or being at risk
of being, physically or sexually abused or ill
treated or a parent has behaved
in such a way towards the child that the child has suffered or is at risk of
suffering serious psychological
points arise:
threshold is low, it arises where there are “reasonable grounds to
suspect” the prescribed matters, i.e. there is
no requirement of any
degree of certainty.
current aspect of the “at risk of harm” definition relates to the
concerns as to the child’s safety, welfare
or well-being. There is no
requirement that there be any perception of on-going risk of the abusive or
harmful behaviour continuing.
any view of the evidence here, the disclosure on 21 October 2003, in the context
of an already vulnerable child with a complex
family situation, were such that
there were current concerns for Miss B’s safety, welfare and well being by
reason of potentially
harmful and/or abusive behaviour by Mr A. The case notes
are littered with expressions of concerns for Miss B’s safety and
and those concerns were the factors precipitating the referral to Dr
Brechman-Toussaint. Indeed the Dene/Donnelly discharge
summary specifically
refers to the appreciation in October 2003 of the harm caused to Miss B by Mr
the obligation is on those who deliver health care or welfare services wholly or
partly to children in the course of their
professional work. Ms Dene, in
providing psychological services to Mr A and to Miss B and Miss C was clearly
doing so in her position
as a member of a health care profession. She did so
pursuant to her professional registration. She was clearly subject to the
obligation in
section 27.
there can be no doubt that from October 2003 her professional remit encompassed
the interests of Miss B. She was in the
course of her professional work
providing services that were partly to Miss B.
any event, and even if payment were relevant (which it is not under
appears from the contract tendered by Dr Donnelly at R2, and from Dr
Donnelly’s evidence, Ms Dene was in fact paid for work
including some (or
possibly all) of her work with Mr A. Ms Dene herself accepted that she was paid
for the dementia practicum and
received a stipend for the better outcomes in
mental health

observations on the facts

previous few years, ceasing only 2 weeks prior to his disclosure, and
that he was aware that he had inappropriate sexual thoughts
including attraction to her.
was or ought to have been clear to her that this was abuse.

Ms Dene’s demeanour and
manner during her oral evidence
Dene’s demeanour and manner whilst giving her oral evidence displayed
considerable volatility and variation.
times, her reaction was one of high emotions. On occasion she showed that the
events that gave rise to these proceedings continued

other times Ms Dene was entirely composed and even argumentative in responding

Candour and Insight of Ms Dene
are number of features of the evidence which reflect poorly upon the candour and
insight of Ms Dene.
Ms Dene repeatedly sought to minimise the nature of the disclosure of which she
was in fact aware in October 2003, and of other
matters arising
thereafter.
does not reflect well on her credit.
Ms Dene repeatedly sought to deflect responsibility onto Dr Donnelly and/or Dr
Brechman-Toussaint, including asserting that
relevant ethical obligations were
on Dr Donnelly and not upon
This displays
a lack of insight into the critical nature of her own professional obligations
as a conditionally registered psychologist
providing psychological treatment and
management to Mr A. As set out by Dr Banks, she remained an individual capable
of asserting
herself, seeking additional information, and acting
unilaterally.
Ms Dene in these proceedings has sought to minimise the nature of her own
responsibility by reference to her own psychological
vulnerability around the
relevant period. However, this vulnerability was clearly apparent to Ms Dene and
she chose to continue treating
Mr A. The Tribunal should not find that Ms
Dene’s previous experiences were such that she should be excused from her
responsibility
for her failures. Rather, it appears that Ms Dene allowed her
desire herself to help Mr A, and Miss B and Miss C, to cloud her professional
judgment to an unacceptable degree. It was her professional judgment that the
risk of ongoing abuse was low, and that the potential
benefits she personally
could bring to the family were sufficient to justify her course of action in not
reporting to DoCS. As she
put it in her witness statement at [21]:
decided to continue treatment as I believed that Mr A’s daughter
would benefit from my assistance to her father.”
precisely the sort of analysis which is warned against in the APS Guidelines.
this case, Ms Dene’s inability over a period of 14 months to complete
records of the attendances on Mr A between 21 and 23
October 2003 is the
clearest possible indicator that throughout this period Ms Dene was well aware
of the nature and seriousness
of the disclosure, and her own inability to

Unsatisfactory
Professional Conduct – Complaint One
definition of unsatisfactory professional conduct, equivalent to that in s 25 of
the Act, was considered by the Medical Tribunal
In re a Medical
Practitioner and the Medical Practice Act
(3 September 2007). The changes to
the previous definition were described in paragraph 10 as being twofold: first
in introducing
the concept of different levels of experience and skill requiring
different standards, and secondly, in introducing the idea that
the conduct had
to be “significantly below” the standard to be so expected. At
paragraph 12 “significantly”
was said to mean “not trivial, of
importance, or substantial”.
matters set out above, individually and cumulatively, clearly establish
unsatisfactory professional conduct. The standard required,
having regard to
professional training, the Code, s 27, and the guidelines of the Australian
Psychological Society clearly demanded
a standard of conduct of which Ms Dene
fell far short.
Professional Misconduct – Complaint
24 of the Act provides that professional misconduct is unsatisfactory
professional conduct of a sufficiently serious nature
to justify suspension or
cancellation of the psychologists registration. Whilst the statutory definition
of professional misconduct
makes it unnecessary to turn to the common law
definition, it was held in
Prothonotary of Supreme Court of NSW v. McCaffery
[2004] NSWCA 470
, that it gives content to a statutory definition of
professional misconduct that it was enacted in the context that
“professional
misconduct” at common law was said “to connote
“conduct which would reasonably be regarded as disgraceful or
dishonourable”
by one’s peers: see
Allinson v General Council of
Medical Education and Registration
[1894] 1 QB 750
and other authorities
collected by Spigelman CJ (with whom Mason P and Handley JA agreed) in
South Wales Bar Association v Cummins
[2001] NSWCA
(2001) 52 NSWLR 279
the nature and potential risks associated with the conduct of Ms Dene as set out
above, her conduct was professional misconduct
within the meaning of s 24 of the
Act. Of particular significance in this regard is:

HCCC v DENE & DONNELLY
SUBMISSION TO THE PSYCHOLOGIST’S TRIBUNAL OF NEW
SOUTH WALES
ON BEHALF OF MS KERRI DENE
INTRODUCTION
matter proceeded to Hearing before the Psychologist’s Tribunal of NSW
(‘the Tribunal’) on 15 to 19 June inclusive
and, 17 and 18 August
2009. The Tribunal was constituted by the Chairperson, Ms Joanne Muller, two
psychologists, Dr E Tong and
Mr C Taylor and a layperson, Ms F Taylor.
to the commencement of the Hearing a number of Directions Hearings and informal
discussions between the parties took place in
respect of the conduct and
management of this and the related matter involving Dr J Donnelly. At the
Direction of the Chairperson,
the 2 matters proceeded to Hearing together.
the Hearing dealt with the conduct of both Ms Dene and Dr Donnelly, this
submission is confined only to the conduct of Ms Dene.
the course of the Directions Hearing on 7 May 2009, counsel for Ms Dene
indicated to the Chairperson that Ms Dene admitted
the substance of the conduct
that led to her coming before the Tribunal although did not admit that such
conduct amounted to unsatisfactory
professional conduct or professional
misconduct.
that regard we note the transcript of that Directions Hearing in which
admissions in relation to each of the particulars of the
complaints regarding Ms
Dene were answered by counsel at the request of the Chairperson. We refer
particularly to page 9, line 15
of the transcript:
‘The timeline – the history of the counselling
provided by Ms Dene and what she did, a great deal, 95% of it I think is
admitted in terms of conduct, there would be no controversy there as to findings
of facts as to what was done and what was said in
Ms Dene’s case because
she has conceded most of it. There is no great double handling of information.
There will not be some
longwinded cross-examination of people as to who said and
did what because a great deal if that is settled, with respect.’
LEGAL FRAMEWORK
matter came before the Tribunal consequent upon a Complaint from the Health Care
Complaints Commission (‘HCCC’) in
accordance with
Psychologist’s Act 2001 (‘the Act’). Relevantly, section 34
of the Act states that:
34 Role of the Commission
(1) Before the Board deals with or refers a complaint
under this Part, the Board and the Commission must consult in order to see if
agreement can be reached between them as to the course of action to be taken
concerning a complaint.
(2) Division 2 of
Care Complaints
applies to the consultation and the outcomes of the consultation.
HCCCs Complaint, dated 26 June 2008 is divided into two particularised
Complaints. The First Complaint alleges Ms Dene:
‘Has been guilty of unsatisfactory professional conduct
within the meaning of s.25 of the Act, in that she has:
(i) Demonstrated that the knowledge skill or judgment possessed, or care
exercised, by the psychologist in the practice of psychology
is significantly
below the standard reasonably expected of an equivalent level of training or
experience and/or
(ii) Engaged in improper or unethical conduct in the course of the practice
or purported practice of psychology.’
second Complaint alleges Ms Dene:
‘Has been guilty of professional misconduct in accordance
with section 24 of the Act, in that she has:
(i) Demonstrated that the knowledge skill or judgment possessed, or care
exercised, by the psychologist in the practice of psychology
is significantly
below the standard reasonably expected of an equivalent level of training or
experience and/or
(ii) Engaged in improper or unethical conduct in the course of the practice
or purported practice of psychology.’
note the assertion of the HCCC at paragraph 3 of its submission namely,
the evidence clearly establishes, to the requisite standard of the
balance of probabilities
.’ Whilst such a standard may well suit the
HCCC, it is not with respect the appropriate test. As the Tribunal would be
aware, the appropriate test in matters such as this is that espoused by Sir
Owen Dixon in
Briginshaw v Briginshaw
[1938] HCA 34
‘But reasonable satisfaction is not a state of mind that
is attained or established independently of the nature and consequence
fact or facts to be proved. The seriousness of an allegation made, the inherent
unlikelihood of an occurrence of a given description,
or the gravity of the
consequences flowing from a particular finding are considerations which must
affect the answer to the question
whether the Issue has been proved to the
reasonable satisfaction of the tribunal. In such matters 'reasonable
satisfaction' should
not be produced by inexact proofs, indefinite testimony, or
indirect inferences. ...’
the Tribunal would also be aware, the Act (along with other Health related
Legislation) was amended following the Special Commission
of Inquiry into
concerns in respect of the Camden and Campbelltown Hospitals by Special
Commissioner Walker SC. One of the amendments
to the Act was to introduce the
words ‘significantly below’ into the definition of
‘Unsatisfactory Professional
Conduct.’ Those words are not defined
in the Act although some guidance as to their operation may be gained from the
Reading Speech when the legislation was introduced into Parliament
‘The first main purpose of the bill is
to refocus the
Health Care Complaints Commission (HCCC) on investigating serious complaints
. To achieve this, Commissioner Walker SC
recommended that unsatisfactory professional conduct be redefined so that only
significant
instances involving lack of skill, judgment, or care will result in
an investigation or disciplinary action. Unsatisfactory professional
will be defined as.....the reference to 'significantly' in that context may
refer to a single act or omission that demonstrates
a practitioner's lack of
skill, judgment or care, or it may refer to a pattern of conduct. In any
individual case, that will depend
on seriousness of the circumstances of the
’ (Our emphasis)
phrase ‘significantly below’ was also Judicially considered in the
Re A Medical Practitioner and the Medical Practice Act
2007] where Judge Freeman stated:
‘As a general principle, the use of the term
significant’ may in law be taken to mean not trivial, of importance, or
substantial.’
remarks of course, focus the decision-makers attention upon the nature of the
evidence required to establish the case. They
do not change the standard of
proof - which remains that of reasonable satisfaction as outlined above.
our submission the wording of the definition of ‘unsatisfactory
professional conduct’ is telling. As such, we submit
that for the
Tribunal to find unsatisfactory professional conduct in respect of Ms Dene the
conduct particularised in the Complaint
must be conduct that is
significantly
reasonably expected of a psychologist of an equivalent level
of training.
With respect, both aspects of that definition must be met.
our submission and, notwithstanding the admissions made by Ms Dene prior to and
during the Hearing, her conduct does not fall within
the definition of
‘significantly below’ that reasonable expected of a psychologist of
an equivalent level of training
or experience. Importantly, we note the
level of specific training. Ms Dene obtained the degree of Bachelor of
Psychology (Hons) in 1999. Between 1994 and
1999, she worked as a mediator
and/or counsellor on a part-time / sessional basis. (Tab 1, RA) Prior to
commencing her Degree in
Clinical Psychology at the Regional University in 2002,
Ms Dene had not conducted a professional clinical psychology session with
Ms Dene was
undertaking a practicum through the Regional University at the relevant
Ms Dene was
supervised by two senior psychologist’s (both having been conferred with
the Degree of PhD) and, one of whom was
responsible for teaching the child
psychology course at the University;
Ms Dene held
‘conditional’ and not full Registration.
our primary submission, the Tribunal would not be satisfied that Ms Dene’s
conduct the subject of this matter amounts to
Unsatisfactory Professional
for the Tribunal to find Ms Dene guilty of professional misconduct, that conduct
would have to be of a
‘sufficiently serious nature’
justify suspension or cancellation or registration.
we submit the Tribunal would not be satisfied on a proper review of the evidence
before it that Ms Dene’s conduct amounted
to Professional Misconduct.

Dene was extremely concerned to ensure that she fulfill the legal obligations

Children and Young Persons
(Care and Protection) Act 1998

sought guidance and reassurance as to that decision from her 2
supervisor’s, both
of whom formed the view that in the circumstances, none
of them were mandatory reporters and neither was there a requirement or need
make a referral to DoCS.
our submission, a supervisee (Ms Dene in this case) must be entitled to rely
upon the direction and guidance of his or her supervisors
(Dr Donnelly and Dr
Brechman-Toussaint in this case) at the time. With respect, if it subsequently
becomes apparent that such advice
or guidance was erroneous, then the
responsibility for that should not be visited upon the supervisee. Indeed, in
our submission,
the supervisee would have a more difficult task seeking to
defend a position wherein he or she sought advice or guidance and thereafter
simply disregarded it.
Notwithstanding
that, Ms Dene was not of the view that there was a ‘current risk’ to
Mr A’s children (neither were
Dr Donnelly and Dr Brechman-Toussaint) nor
with respect, was Ms Dene providing services to children. The documentation
by DoCS is entirely consistent with such an understanding. In that
regard we refer to the DoCS document, ‘Do you work with
people?’ dated November 2000, within the documents provided by the HCCC at
Volume 3, Tab 5.
That document explicitly states that:
‘A mandatory reporter is someone who is required by law to make a
report to DoCS if they have current concerns about the safety,
wellbeing of a child. A child is a person under 16 years.’
Ms Dene did not have current concerns for [Miss B]’s welfare or wellbeing
neither with respect, did Dr Donnelly and Dr
Brechman-Toussaint.

‘Any person who delivers the following
services to
as part of their
Dene was not providing services to children nor, in relation to Mr A was she in

THE HCCCs SUBMISSIONS
first with the HCCCs invitation to the Tribunal that it ought to ‘
as referred to it and deal with an additional complaint that Ms Dene suffers
from an impairment that detrimentally affects or
is likely to detrimentally
affect her mental capacity to practise psychology, namely Post Traumatic Stress
’, we note the following.
the HCCC initially contacted Ms Dene in respect of this matter in May 2006. Ms
Dene provided written responses to the HCCC
on 22 June 2006, 23 August 2006, 23
February 2007 and through her lawyers on 24 September 2007. In her response to
the HCCC dated
23 August 2006, Ms Dene confirmed that she disclosed her own
circumstances to Mr A upon his disclosure to her. Indeed, she said,
was very distressed
’. Further, Ms Dene indicated in her statement
which was filed in these proceedings on 30 April 2009 at paragraphs 16, 17 and
18 that she was inter alia, distressed and teary, extremely distressed and in
shock and crying.
Ms Boland said in her report dated 14 April 2009 at page 5, ‘
she should receive our compassion. It was after all, very bad luck for her to
be faced with such a personally confronting
issue so early in her training.
Added to this the support and guidance that she appears to have been denied
later compounded her
we note the comments of Ms Zanderberg at page 8 (un-numbered) of her report
this experience has shattered Ms Dene’s personal and
professional life. A life she has made painstaking steps to reenter, and
the HCCC sought and was granted leave on two (2) occasions during the course of
the Hearing to amend its Complaint dated
26 June 2008. Indeed, the second
occasion on which leave was granted to amend the complaint was on the fourth day
of a Hearing listed
for 5 days.
the fact that Ms Dene did not react well to the initial disclosure by Mr A and
thereafter has found it incredibly difficult
and confronting to deal with this
matter, has with respect been known to the HCCC since as early as August 2006
and, in any event,
since it received Ms Dene’s documents in April 2009.
respect, to wait until well after the conclusion of a Hearing to make such a
submission to the Tribunal and bearing in mind all
of the evidence is plainly
outrageous.
we readily accept the Tribunal has the power to amend the Complaint or make a
Complaint of its own, we note that power extends
to matters which
might arise
during the course of any such proceedings
. (Schedule 5, Clause 2 of the Act)
With respect, nothing has arisen during the course of the Hearing which was
either not known to
the HCCC at the time or before.
the circumstances, we submit that to even entertain such an invitation, let
alone act upon it, would be a gross denial of procedural
fairness and ought to
be denied forthwith.
next with the HCCCs submission generally, we most strongly urge the Tribunal to
read the HCCCs submission and to have close
regard not only to the various
purported transcript references and other evidence provided for within the
footnotes but, to also
review very closely the primary source document to which
the HCCC seeks to refer. With respect, the Tribunal must do that to enable
itself to be satisfied that the references to certain evidence actually refer to
that evidence in full.
note the HCCCs submissions regarding Ms Dene’s presentation during the
course of the Hearing. We accept Ms Dene displayed
considerable distress during
the course of the Hearing. As the Tribunal is well aware, Ms Dene has been the
subject of investigation
for over six years through various bodies and that
cannot be disregarded when assessing her demeanor.
also note Ms Dene remained under cross-examination during the 8 week adjournment
of the matter and, was unable to speak with anyone
to her, nor to seek reassurance from any persons in respect of this matter
including her lawyers. If
for no other reason that alone was of great
significance.
and as indicated above, Ms Dene in no way wishes to seek to deflect her
responsibilities and we submit she made that patently
clear throughout her
evidence. Indeed, we submit her statement and oral evidence clearly confirms
that she is regretful and possesses
insight into her actions. Ms Boland agreed
that Ms Dene’s reason and explanation were not a deflection and upon
questioning
she stated (Transcript, page 57, lines 45-50, 18 June 2009):
‘Q (Ainsworth): So that all the factors you’ve
listed – and I think you’ve given us a very comprehensive picture
– are explanations as to why Ms Dene acted in the was she
Q: Rather than an excuse for her behavior.
A: Oh, yes.’
The Referral and Treatment of Mr A
refer to paragraph 7 of the HCCCs submissions. The assertion that Ms Dene and
Dr Donnelly ‘agreed’ that Ms Dene continue
to treat Mr A is
incorrect even on the wording of the transcript. We note Ms Dene’s
response (page 9, line 22 of the Transcript,
19 June 2009):
“I understood that I was accruing practicum hours ready of
the next practicum. That’s what Dr Donnelly told me.”
agree with the HCCCs submission that Ms Dene’s treatment of Mr A was her
long term clinical psychological interaction
also note that this was Dr Donnelly’s first supervision of a student
undertaking a long term clinical psychological
interaction.
refer to paragraph 20 of the HCCCs submissions and to the assertion that it
ought to have been clear to her that this was abuse
respect, that is an entirely self-serving and inappropriate submission to make.
Ms Dene provided evidence that is
was not clear to her. Ms Dene sought
assistance and clarification from her supervisors. With respect, what ought to
have been clear

HCCC V DENE & DONNELLY
HCCC SUBMISSIONS IN REPLY RE: DENE
Introduction
HCCC has already served lengthy and detailed submissions in this matter.
the extent that the submissions served on behalf of Ms Dene take issue with
those submissions, those matters remain in issue. It
is not proposed in any way
to repeat matters by way of reply.
reply submissions deal only with discrete points upon which it may assist the
Tribunal for the Commission to respond to matters
raised on behalf of Ms
Standard of Proof
standard of proof is the civil standard as stated in
Briginshaw v.
[1938] HCA 34
(1983) 60 CLR 336
at 362-3. However by reason of the seriousness
of the allegations and the gravity of the consequences the Tribunal must be
comfortably
satisfied that the matters in the complaint have been
established.
Court of Criminal Appeal has recently cautioned against the use of
“comfortably satisfied” as implying that proof
to a higher standard
than the balance of probabilities is required:
Gianoutsos v Glykis
NSWLR 539 at 547-9 applied by the Medical Tribunal in
In re Dr Suman Sood
[2006] NSWMT 1
at 10. More recently, in
Polglaze v Veterinary Practitioners
Board of NSW
[2009] NSWSC 347
, the court has confirmed that at
common law there are only two standards of proof, the ordinary civil standard of
proof upon a balance
of probabilities and the criminal standard of proof beyond
reasonable doubt. The
standard is not a third standard or
proof. Rather it is concerned with the quality or sufficiency of the evidence
necessary to discharge
the civil standard.
The Responsibility of
the Supervisee
set out in the HCCC’s closing submissions, a psychologist in Ms
Dene’s position in October 2003, and at all times up
to and including
August 2005, was subject to ethical and professional obligations notwithstanding
her role as an intern. Contrary
to the submission on behalf of Ms Dene at [20],
she was not entitled to rely upon the direction and guidance of her supervisor
was required to exercise her own individual judgment.
all times, to her knowledge, if she had any uncertainty as to the legal
obligations upon her she had access to the DOCS helpline.
is submitted that the submission that
“Ms Dene did not have current
concerns for Miss B’s welfare or wellbeing”
at [22] flies in the
face of the clinical notes completed by Ms Dene. It also flies in the face of Ms

at 41/40-47 that Ms Dene had concerns for the
psychological welfare of Miss B from the moment Mr A made his disclosure in

is accepted that Dr Donnelly made the referral to Dr Brechman-Toussaint, however
Ms Dene should be regarded as jointly referring
Miss B and Miss C to Dr
Brechman-Toussaint and as responsible (largely because she was the recipient of
relevant information from
Mr A, for the communication to Dr Brechman-Toussaint
of relevant treatment information).
set out in the HCCC’s submissions, the information provided to Dr
Brechman-Toussaint was incomplete.

Tribunal to treat an additional complaint as referred

having regard to the evidence and demeanour of

Ms Dene’s impairment and/or its ongoing effect was

the extent that the submissions on behalf of Ms Dene rely upon an absence of
procedural fairness, the HCCC has indicated that submissions

Dene’s representatives as to what procedure the Tribunal should adopt. The

satisfy the requirements of procedural fairness in the individual case (cf

Suppression
Dene is seeking a suppression order. The HCCC has a statutory duty under s94B(2)
of the HCCC Act to make publicly available information
whose registration has been cancelled or suspended. Moreover, as a matter of
generality there is a public interest
in proceedings such as these being public,
including any determination or protective orders. However, it is accepted that
the Tribunal
has, in the course of this hearing, considered sensitive personal

protect sensitive personal information relating solely to events occurring

to limit any award of costs to less than the actual

Introduction
this matter the Health Care Complaints Commission (HCCC) makes two complaints as
regards the conduct of Dr Donnelly from around
October 2003 until August 2005.
Dr Donnelly is a registered psychologist with a certificate of registration
section 123
Psychologists Act 2001
(the Act) valid until 30 June
2010. Each complaint comprises eight separate particulars. One complaint is of

complaints should be found proved. The evidence clearly establishes, to the
requisite standard of the balance of probabilities,
that Dr Donnelly’s
conduct is unsatisfactory professional conduct within section 25, in that the
knowledge, skill or judgment
possessed, or care exercised, by Dr Donnelly in his
practice of psychology was significantly below the standard reasonably expected
of an equivalent level of training or experience and/or he engaged in improper
or unethical conduct in the course of the practice
or purported practice of
psychology, and this was of a sufficiently serious nature to justify suspension
or cancellation of his registration.
Submissions as to appropriate protective
orders are set out below.
Donnelly is a registered psychologist in New South Wales. He graduated from the
University of California at Irvine with a Bachelor
of Science in 1985, and
completed a Master of Science and a Doctorate in Clinical Psychology at
Vanderbilt University, concluding
in 2000. According to his Statement dated 11

of sexual abuse, and had himself completed 5 clinical practica
comprising approximately 4000 supervised hours. From 2001 to 2006

obligation under

Children and Young Persons (Care and

respectively) are clear. They require that a

person in the course of his professional work delivers or supervises the
provision of health care or welfare services wholly or
partly to children. That
unquestionably covers Dr Donnelly both as regards his practice in general, and
as regards this particular
person has reasonable grounds, arising during the course of or from his work, to
suspect that a child is at risk of harm, meaning
concerns exist for the safety, welfare or well-being of the child; and
concerns are because of one or more of the child having been, or being at risk
of being, physically or sexually abused or ill
treated or a parent has behaved
in such a way towards the child that the child has suffered or is at risk of
suffering serious psychological
points arise:
threshold is low, it arises where there are “reasonable grounds to
suspect” the prescribed matters, i.e. there is
no requirement of any
degree of certainty.
current aspect of the “at risk of harm” definition relates to the
concerns as to the child’s safety, welfare
or well-being. There is no
requirement that there be any perception of on-going risk of the abusive or
harmful behaviour continuing.
any view of the evidence here, the disclosure on 21 October 2003, in the context
of an already vulnerable child with a complex
family situation, were such that
there were current concerns for Miss B’s safety, welfare and well being by
reason of potentially

and those concerns were the factors precipitating the referral to Dr Brechman

appreciation in October 2003 of the harm caused to Miss B by Mr A’s past
actions (HCCC vol
there can be no doubt that from October 2003 Dr Donnelly’s professional
remit encompassed the interests of Miss B.

professional work providing services which were partly to Miss B.
Preliminary
observations on the facts
and Extent of the Disclosure by Mr A in October 2003.
The characterisation of the initial disclosure by Mr A to Ms Dene, and of any
subsequent disclosures, is of critical importance to
an evaluation of the
complaints as regards Dr Donnelly. Having regard to all of the evidence, the
likelihood is that it was quite
clear to both Ms Dene and Dr Donnelly from
October 21 2003 that Mr A had deliberately touched Miss B’s pubic region
