Unsatisfactory
Professional Conduct – Complaint One
definition of unsatisfactory professional conduct, equivalent to that in s 25 of
the Act, was considered by the Medical Tribunal
In re a Medical
Practitioner and the Medical Practice Act
(3 September 2007). The changes to
the previous definition were described in paragraph 10 as being twofold: first
in introducing
the concept of different levels of experience and skill requiring
different standards, and secondly, in introducing the idea that
the conduct had
to be “significantly below” the standard to be so expected. At
paragraph 12 “significantly”
was said to mean “not trivial, of
importance, or substantial”.
matters set out above, individually and cumulatively, clearly establish
unsatisfactory professional conduct. The standard required,
having regard to
professional training, the Code, s 27, and the guidelines of the Australian
Psychological Society clearly demanded
a standard of conduct of which Dr
Donnelly fell far short.
Professional
Misconduct – Complaint Two
24 of the Act provides that professional misconduct is unsatisfactory
professional conduct of a sufficiently serious nature
to justify suspension or
cancellation of the psychologist's registration. Whilst the statutory definition
of professional misconduct
makes it unnecessary to turn to the common law
definition, it was held in
Prothonotary of Supreme Court of NSW v. McCaffery
[2004] NSWCA 470
, that it gives content to a statutory definition of
professional misconduct that it was enacted in the context that
“professional
misconduct” at common law was said “to connote
“conduct which would reasonably be regarded as disgraceful or
dishonourable”
by one’s peers: see
Allinson v General Council of
Medical Education and Registration
[1894] 1 QB 750
and other authorities
collected by Spigelman CJ (with whom Mason P and Handley JA agreed) in
South Wales Bar Association v Cummins
[2001] NSWCA
(2001) 52 NSWLR 279
the nature and potential risks associated with the conduct of Dr Donnelly as set
out above, his conduct was professional misconduct
within the meaning of s 24 of
the Act. Of particular significance in this regard is:
failure to review Ms Dene’s clinical records relating to Mr A;
failure to protect the welfare of a child whose circumstances were such that the
vulnerability inherent in childhood was severely
exacerbated;
Donnelly’s failure to conduct any proper risk assessment as regards Miss
Donnelly’s failure to revisit his risk assessment as regards the risk to
Donnelly’s encouragement to Ms Dene to continue treating Mr A when he
ought to have known that this would carry considerable
weight with her;
Donnelly’s willingness to leave Ms Dene in a position of considerable
professional vulnerability without ensuring vigilant
and thorough
supervision;
inadequacy of the referral to Dr Brechman-Toussaint;
inadequate risk assessment;
failures properly to reassess risk or his initial decision not to report Mr A,
including having read and signed up to the discharge
actions in causing or permitting the joint session with Miss B and Mr

is trite law in proceedings such as the present that due to seriousness of the
allegations and the gravity of their consequences
the Tribunal must be
comfortably satisfied
that the particulars of a complaint have been
established (
HCCC v Bryan
[2009] PSTNSW 1 at [11]). (emphasis added)
Donnelly agrees with the submission of the HCCC that the characterisation of the

Ms Dene is of
to the evaluation of the complaints as regards Dr
seriousness of the allegations made against Dr Donnelly cannot possibly be
understated.

Mr A had said;
information given to him by Ms Dene
in the terms as the HCCC

disclosure was to the effect that Mr A had massaged his daughter and sometimes
during that massage his hand had brushed or

HCCC’s case, or a vast proportion of it, is, effectively, that Mr A told
Ms Dene on 21 October 2003 that he had deliberately
touched his

that regard, the HCCC’s case against Dr Donnelly can be broadly distilled
as follows:

‘massaging her pubic region’) his daughter;
(b) Ms Dene
relayed that information to Dr Donnelly;
(c) Despite Dr

report this conduct to DoCS, but rather
made the decision to allow two young

(d) In doing

(g) Completely
disregarded his professional and legal obligations; and
(h) Ms Dene and
Dr Donnelly are lying about what Mr A said to Ms Dene on 21 October 2003.
only needs to consider the above distillation of the case to appreciate the

. In that regard, his Honour Dixon

which more is said below):
“The seriousness of an allegation made, the inherent
unlikelihood of an occurrence of a given description, or the gravity of
consequences flowing from a particular finding are considerations which must
affect the answer to the question whether the issue
has been proved to the
reasonable satisfaction of the tribunal. In such matters ‘reasonable
satisfaction’
should not be produced by inexact proofs, indefinite
testimony, or indirect inferences
... This does not mean that some standard
of persuasion is fixed intermediate between the satisfaction beyond reasonable
doubt required
upon a criminal inquest and the reasonable satisfaction which in
a civil issue may, not must, be based on a preponderance of probability.
means that the nature of the issue necessarily affects the process by which
reasonable satisfaction is attained.
” (emphasis added).

HCCC v DENE & DONNELLY
HCCC SUBMISSIONS IN REPLY RE: DONNELLY
Introduction
HCCC has already served lengthy and detailed submissions in this matter.
the extent that the submissions served on behalf of Dr Donnelly take issue with
those submissions, those matters remain in issue.
It is not proposed in any way
to repeat matters by way of reply.
reply submissions deal only with discrete points upon which it may assist the
Tribunal for the Commission to respond to matters
raised on behalf of Dr
The HCCC’s case
if the disclosure by Mr A to Dr Donnelly, and the information provided by Ms
Dene, was of a lesser nature than the HCCC’s
primary case as set out in
the HCCC’s closing submissions, it is submitted that the complaint is
proved and that the protective
orders should be as set out in those submissions.
It is clear from the evidence that massage behaviour is potentially abusive, and
in the present case the circumstances of that behaviour including the pubic
region and being felt to be somewhat taboo (17/8/09 at
77.35-41) would have been
sufficient to give rise to the professional requirements upon Dr Donnelly as set
out in the HCCC’s
closing submissions.
it would be open to the Tribunal to find that Dr Donnelly has minimised the full
extent of the initial and subsequent disclosures,
albeit without any awareness
that he was doing so. It would equally be open to the Tribunal to find that his
recollection was incomplete.
Reliance upon the Code
has been clear to Dr Donnelly and his representatives that he is subject to the
Code and that the Code, without any particularisation
by reference to individual
paragraphs, is relied upon in these proceedings. It was made clear to Mr Darams
that if he sought to do
so he could recall witnesses to ameliorate any
perception of unfairness on his part. He undertook to consider that, but chose
to recall any witness (18/6/09 98:21-45). Further, the whole of the code may
be relevant in determining whether or not the particularised
breach as set out
in the relevant subparagraph of the complaint is made
Reliability of the notes
context for the Tribunal’s consideration of the reliability of the
clinical notes, as reported by Ms Dene, includes:
likelihood that the more contemporaneous the record the more likely that it
would be accurate; and
discrepancies such as those between the current and historical characterisation
of the disclosure are not credibly explicable
on the basis of
“inaccuracy”, or “appalling” note taking as contended by
Information to Dr Brechman-Toussaint
behalf of Dr Donnelly it is suggested that the nature of the relevant
information which it is alleged that Dr Donnelly should have
provided to Dr
Brechman-Toussaint is unclear ([87]). That suggestion is rejected.
Brechman-Toussaint gave evidence and the Tribunal has her detailed notes. Dr
Donnelly is well aware of the statement by Dr Brechman-Toussaint
that she was
unaware that the touching involved the pubic region. At its lowest, this
particular relates to that information, and
to the information that Mr A felt
that he had broken a taboo in massaging Miss B. Beyond this, the extent of the
failure to disclose
relevant information will depend upon the Tribunal’s
findings as to Dr Donnelly’s knowledge as at this time. There is
unfairness in the particular being framed in that way, and Dr Donnelly has at
all times been well aware of the nature of the complaint
against him in this
Record Keeping
complaint against Dr Donnelly relies upon his failures in keeping clinical
records. Dr Donnelly was cross-examined as to the discharge
summary. There is no
unfairness in relying upon this as an instance of a failure in record keeping
within the particulars of the
